Refuting the Doubt of Directionality in Creation
Imam Nasafi refutes the second doubt by exposing the opponents' hidden belief in divine composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Nasafi refutes the claim that Allah's creation of the world implies direction by showing that the very question reveals a corrupt belief in divine composition.

Core Idea
The inside/outside question itself exposes a false belief in Allah's divisibility.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi tackles a second doubt raised by opponents: if Allah created the world, was it inside or outside His essence? Either way, they argue, directionality is established. Nasafi responds by turning the question back on them, showing that the very concepts of inside and outside only apply to divisible bodies, thereby exposing their hidden belief that Allah is composed of parts. This refutation relies on the logical consequence of their own question.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal translation. Notice how Nasafi addresses the doubt directly.
2
Identify the opponents' argument
The opponents ask: did Allah create the world inside or outside His essence? They claim that whichever it is, direction is implied.
3
Understand Nasafi's counter
Nasafi says this question reveals their hidden belief that Allah is divisible and composite, because the inside/outside distinction only applies to divisible bodies.
4
Trace the two refutation paths
Nasafi provides two ways the proof exposes them: by the statement itself and by the proof. He also notes that the opponents themselves agree Allah cannot be divisible, so this suffices.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبهة الثانية التي تعلقوا بها: أنه تعالى كان ولا عالم ثم خلقه. أخلقه في ذاته؟ أم خارج ذاته؟ وكيف ما كان فقد تحققت الجهة. فنقول - والله إن هذا شيء بينتم على ما تضمرون من عقيدتكم الفاسدة أنه تعالى متبعض متجزئ، وإن كنتم تتبرؤن منه عند قيام الدلالة على بطلان تلك المقالة، وتزعمون أنا نعني بالجسم القيام بالذات وهذه المسألة بنفس المقالة. وما تتمسكون به من الدلالة تهتك عليكم ما أسبلتم من أستاركم، ويبدي عن مكنون أسراركم إما بنفس المقالة، فلأن شغل جميع العرش مع عظمته لن يكون إلا بمتبعض متجزئ على ما قررنا، وإما بالدلالة فلأن الداخل والخارج لن يكون إلا بما هو متبعض متجزئ وقيام الدلالة وانضمام ظاهر إجماعكم على بطلان ذلك يغنينا عن إفساد هذه الشبهة، والله الموفق.
As for the second doubt which they clung to: that Allah Most High was and there was no world, then He created it. Did He create it in His essence or outside His essence? Whichever it is, direction is established. So we say - by Allah, this is something you have disclosed from what you conceal of your corrupt belief that Allah Most High is divisible and partitionable, even if you disavow it when proof of the falsity of that statement is established, and you claim that we mean by body (jism) self-subsistence. This issue is itself that statement. And what you cling to as proof tears open the curtain you have let down, and reveals your hidden secrets: either by the statement itself, for occupying the entire Throne despite its greatness would only be by a divisible and partitionable being as we have established; or by the proof, for entering and exiting can only be for what is divisible and partitionable. And the establishment of proof and the addition of your apparent consensus on its falsity suffices us from refuting this doubt. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage refutes the second doubt (shubha) raised by the opponents: since Allah existed alone and then created the world, either He created it within His own essence or outside it, which would imply a direction (jiha). The author responds that this very question reveals the opponents' hidden belief that Allah is divisible (mutabaghghid) and composed of parts (mutajazzi'), because the concepts of 'inside' and 'outside' only apply to divisible bodies. Even if they pretend to mean by 'body' merely self-subsistence, the issue remains. The proof itself exposes them: occupying the entire Throne would require division, and the act of entering or exiting is only possible for a divisible entity. Since even the opponents agree that Allah cannot be divisible, this suffices to refute the doubt.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The categories of inside and outside only apply to divisible, spatial beings, not to Allah who is indivisible.

Key Takeaway

Refuting the Inside/Outside Dilemma: Allah's Transcendence
Imam Nasafi refutes the claim that Allah is inside or outside the world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why spatial categories like 'inside' and 'outside' do not apply to Allah, using the analogy of an accident in a substance.

Core Idea
Existence does not imply location; only bodies have inside/outside.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi addresses a clever objection: if Allah is existent, He must be either inside or outside the world. Since He is not inside, He must be outside—implying direction. Nasafi exposes the flaw: these terms only apply to divisible bodies. He uses the analogy of an accident (arad) subsisting in a substance, which is not described as inside or outside. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the flipped argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the opponents turn the earlier argument around to claim Allah must be outside the world.
The opponents' argument is based on the assumption that existence implies location.
2
Understand the refutation's core principle
Nasafi states that only divisible bodies (jism mutabaghghid mutajazzi) can be described as inside or outside. Understand why this is the key.
This principle is derived from the nature of bodies and spatial relations.
3
Examine the analogy with the accident
Look at the analogy of an accident subsisting in a substance. Why does it show that inside/outside don't apply? Apply the same reasoning to Allah.
The accident is not a body, so it is not described as inside or outside. Similarly, Allah is not a body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وربما يقلبون هذا الكلام ويقولون بأنه تعالى لما كان موجودا إما أن يكون داخل العالم، وإما أن يكون خارج العالم، وليس بداخل العالم فكان خارجا منه، وهذا يوجب كونه بجهة منه. والجواب عن هذا الكلام على نحو ما أجبنا عن الشبهة المتقدمة أن الموصوف بالدخول والخروج هو الجسم المتبعض المتجزئ فأما ما لا تبعض له ولا يتجزأ فلا يوصف بكونه داخلا ولا خارجا، ألا ترى أن العرض القائم بجوهر لا يوصف بكونه داخلا فيه ولا خارجا منه، فكذا القديم لما لم يكن جسما لا يوصف بذلك، فكان هذا الكلام أيضا مبنيا على ما يضمرون من عقيدتهم الفاسدة.
And sometimes they turn this speech around and say that since Allah Most High was existent, He is either inside the world or outside the world. And He is not inside the world, so He is outside it, and this entails His being in a direction from it. The answer to this speech is similar to what we answered for the previous doubt: that described by entering and exiting is the divisible, partitionable body. As for what has no division and is not partitionable, it is not described as being inside or outside. Do you not see that the accident (al-'arad) subsisting in a substance is not described as being inside it or outside it? Likewise, the Eternal (al-qadim) since He is not a body, is not described as such. So this speech is also built on what they conceal of their corrupt belief.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage addresses a flipped version of the previous argument: the opponents now argue that since Allah is existent, He must be either inside the world or outside it; and since He is not inside (as the world is created), He must be outside, implying direction. The answer repeats the earlier point: the predicates 'inside' and 'outside' only apply to divisible bodies (jism mutabaghghid mutajazzi'). For a being that is not divisible, these terms are inapplicable. The author uses the analogy of an accident (arad) subsisting in a substance (jawhar) — the accident is not described as inside or outside the substance. Similarly, the Eternal (al-qadim) is not a body, so these terms do not apply. Thus, the argument is based on the opponents' hidden corrupt belief that Allah is corporeal.

Key Terms

خارج
عرض
جوهر
قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Existence necessarily implies spatial location (inside/outside).
Correction: Only bodies have location; non-physical beings like Allah are not subject to spatial categories.

Checkpoint
What analogy does Imam Nasafi use to show that inside/outside do not apply to Allah?
A) The soul in the body.
B) The accident subsisting in a substance.
C) The universe as a whole.
D) The concept of time.

Key Takeaway
Spatial predicates like 'inside' and 'outside' only apply to bodies; therefore, Allah, being not a body, cannot be described by them.

Learning Objective

Refutation of the Sophism about Occupying the Throne
Refutes a sophism by showing that occupying the Throne requires divisibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the notion of God occupying the Throne is rationally impossible, based on the necessity of divisibility and the inapplicability of spatial categories like inside/outside.

Core Idea
Occupying space requires divisibility; God is indivisible, so He cannot occupy the Throne.

The author now refutes a sophism that claims God occupies the Throne. He shows, through two complementary arguments, that such a notion implies divisibility and spatial limitations that are impossible for the transcendent Creator. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument come through.
Notice the two parts: 'by the statement itself' and 'by the indication'.
2
Understand the first argument
The first argument (bi nafs al-maqāla) states that occupying the entire Throne would only be possible for something divisible and partitionable. This directly refutes the sophism because God is not divisible.
3
Understand the second argument
The second argument (bi al-dalāla) notes that the concepts of inside and outside only apply to divisible things. Since God is not divisible, He cannot be inside or outside the Throne.
This reinforces the first argument from a different angle.
4
Note the appeal to consensus
The author mentions that the opponents' own consensus on the falsehood of such attributes suffices to refute the sophism, without needing further elaboration.
This shows the argument is already accepted by the opponents themselves.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
به من الدلالة تهتك عليكم ما أسبلتم من أستاركم، ويبدي عن مكنون أسراركم إما بنفس المقالة، فلأن شغل جميع العرش مع عظمته لن يكون إلا بمتبعض متجزئ على ما قررنا، وإما بالدلالة فلأن الداخل والخارج لن يكون إلا بما هو متبعض متجزى وقيام الدلالة وانضمام ظاهر إجماعكم على بطلان ذلك يغنينا عن إفساد هذه الشبهة، والله الموفق.
By the evidence, it tears away what you have let down of your veils, and exposes the hidden of your secrets, either by the statement itself: for occupying the entire Throne (al-ʿarsh) with its greatness would only be possible for something divisible and partitionable, as we have established; or by the indication: for the inside and outside would only be for something divisible and partitioned. The establishment of the indication and the addition of the apparent consensus of you on the falsehood of that suffices us from refuting this sophism (shubha). And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage refutes a sophism (shubha) presented by opponents. The author argues in two ways: first, by the statement itself (bi nafs al-maqāla) that if God were to occupy the entire Throne (al-ʿarsh) with its greatness, that would require Him to be divisible (mutabacʿiḍ mutajazziʾ), which is impossible for God. Second, by the indication (bi al-dalāla) that the concepts of inside (dākhil) and outside (khārij) only apply to something divisible and partitioned, which God is not. The author also mentions that the opponents' own consensus on the falsehood of such notions suffices to refute the sophism, without needing further elaboration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: That God can be described by spatial properties like occupying, inside, or outside.
Correction: Spatial categories only apply to divisible, physical objects. God is neither divisible nor physical, so such descriptions are impossible.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

Objection that God is Outside the World and Its Refutation
Presents an objection and refutes it using the accident-substance analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that God must be outside the world is invalid and how the Maturidi response uses the analogy of accident and substance.

Core Idea
Existence does not imply location; only bodies are described as inside or outside.

This passage addresses a common objection raised by those who assume that any existent must be either inside or outside the world. The response clarifies that spatial predicates only apply to bodies, not to the non-corporeal Eternal. The analogy of an accident subsisting in a substance demonstrates that something can exist without being spatially located.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the logical inversion: since God exists and is not inside the world, He must be outside, hence in a direction.
This objection seems plausible if one assumes existence implies spatial location.
2
Identify the key premise of the answer
The answer restricts the predicates 'inside' and 'outside' to divisible, partitioned bodies. God is not a body, so the disjunction does not apply.
3
Examine the analogy of accident and substance
The author uses the example of an accident (ʿaraḍ) subsisting in a substance (jawhar): it is not described as inside or outside. This shows non-bodily existence is possible without location.
This analogy is crucial for understanding the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وربما يقلبون هذا الكلام ويقولون بأنه تعالى لما كان موجودا إما أن يكون داخل العالم، وإما أن يكون خارج العالم، وليس بداخل العالم فكان خارجا منه، وهذا يوجب كونه بجهة منه. والجواب عن هذا الكلام على نحو ما أجبنا عن الشبهة المتقدمة أن الموصوف بالدخول والخروج هو الجسم المتبعض المتجزئ فأما ما لا تبعض له ولا يتجزأ فلا يوصف بكونه داخلا ولا خارجا، ألا ترى أن العرض القائم بجوهر لا يوصف بكونه داخلا فيه ولا خارجا منه، فكذا القديم لما لم يكن جسما لا يوصف بذلك، فكان هذا الكلام أيضا مبنيا على ما يضمرون من عقيدتهم الفاسدة.
And perhaps they invert this statement and say: Since the Exalted is existent, either He is inside the world or outside the world; He is not inside the world, so He is outside it, and this necessitates that He is in a direction (jiha) from it. Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objection is a logical inversion: if God is not inside, He must be outside, hence in a direction. The answer refutes by clarifying that inside/outside are predicates only for bodies. The analogy of accident and substance shows that something can exist without being spatially located. Thus the objection is based on a false premise that existence implies location.

Refuting the Claim of Spatial Necessity for Two Existents
Refutes the third sophism about spatial necessity for two existents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why spatial relationship is not a necessary condition for the existence of two entities, and how the author refutes the sophism using examples from substance and accident.

Core Idea
Two existents need not be spatially related; counterexamples prove the fallacy.

The third sophism claims that two existents are inconceivable without one being in the direction of or beside the other. This sounds plausible, but the author shows it is not a necessary condition. He provides clear examples from everyday entities like substance and accident to demonstrate the flaw.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without commentary. Then read the literal translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Pay attention to the two conditions the sophism imposes: direction (jiha) and adjacency (janb).
2
Identify the sophism and its two claims
The sophism states that two existents must either be in each other's direction or beside each other. Note that these are presented as exhaustive possibilities.
3
Analyze the counterexamples
The author gives two counterexamples: substance and accident (not in each other's direction) and two substances (not beside each other). Understand why each refutes the corresponding claim.
Consider how substance and accident exist without spatial relation.
4
Connect to the Christian division
The author links this refutation to the Christian classification of existents into substance, body, and accident. See how denying that God is body or accident leads to the false conclusion that He is substance.
This shows the broader theological significance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما حل الشبهة الثالثة : وهي أن الموجودين لا يعقلان موجودين إلا وأن يكون أحدهما بجهة صاحبه أو بجنبه. قلنا : هذا منكم تقسيم للموجودين، وليس من ضرورة الوجود أحد الأمرين، لأنهما إن كانا موجودين، كان أحدهما بجهة صاحبه، ينبغي أن لا يكون الجوهر وما قام به من العرض موجودين، لأن أحدهما ليس بجهة صاحبه . وإن كانا موجودين لأن أحدهما بجنب صاحبه ينبغي أن لا يكون الجوهران موجودين لأن أحدهما ليس بحيث صاحبه، وقد ما بطلان هذا من إبطال قول النصارى أن الموجود إما أن يكون جوهرا، وإما أن يكون جسما، وإما أن يكون عرضا . والباري جل وعلا ليس بجسم ولا عرض. فدل أنه جوهر فإن بطل ذلك بطل هذا. وإن صح هذا صح ذلك. بل كلا الأمرين باطل لما مر ، والله الموفق.
As for the resolution of the third sophism: that two existents are not conceivable except that one of them is in the direction of the other or beside it. We say: This is a division of existents from you, but neither of the two is a necessity of existence. For if it were that if two existents exist, one must be in the direction of the other, then a substance (jawhar) and the accident subsisting in it should not be existents, because one of them is not in the direction of the other. And if it were that they exist because one is beside the other, then two substances should not be existents, because one of them is not at the locus (ḥayth) of the other. And this is already invalidated by refuting the Christians' statement that the existent is either substance, or body, or accident. The Creator (al-Bārī) is neither body nor accident, so it follows that He is a substance. If that is false, this is false. If this is true, that is true. Rather, both [divisions] are false due to what has passed. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage resolves a third sophism claiming that two existents must be spatially related. The author refutes it by a counterexample: a substance and its accident are not spatially related (the accident is not in a direction of the substance), yet they are both existent. Similarly, two substances are not necessarily beside each other to exist. The author also connects this to the Christian division of existents into substance, body, accident, and shows that God is neither body nor accident, so if one claims He is substance, that is also false because substances are bodies. So the whole division is invalid.

Key Terms
موجودين
جهة
جنب
جوهر
عرض
جسم
النصارى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two existents must be spatially related (in direction or adjacency).
Correction: The existence of a substance and its accident, or two substances, shows that spatial relation is not necessary.

Checkpoint
What counterexample does the author use to show that two existents need not be spatially related?
A) Two angels
B) A substance and an accident
C) Two bodies in different places
D) God and the Throne

Key Takeaway
The author demonstrates that spatial relationship is not a necessary condition for two existents, using the counterexample of substance and accident, and links it to refuting the Christian division of existents.

Refuting the Necessity of Spatial Relation Between Existents
Refutation of spatial necessity with reductio and Christian doctrine link.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why kalam rejects the claim that existence necessarily entails spatial relation to another existent, and how this refutation also undermines the Christian substance doctrine.

Core Idea
Existence does not require spatial relation; God transcends all direction and place.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi tackles a pervasive assumption: that any two existents must be related by spatial direction or adjacency. He shows that this assumption leads to absurd consequences, and that even the Christian classification of existents into substance, body, and accident fails to contain Allah. This lesson unpacks his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the compressed style and the logical 'if...then' structure.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The passage begins by quoting the opponent: two existents must be either at the direction of each other or beside each other. Understand what this claim implies: it makes spatial relation a necessary condition of existence.
3
Follow the first reductio
The author applies the claim to a substance and its accident: if spatial relation were necessary, then a substance and its accident would not be considered existent because the accident is not at the direction of the substance. This is absurd, so the premise is false.
Substance and accident are co-located, but not 'at the direction' of each other in the usual sense.
4
Follow the second reductio and the Christian link
Similarly, two substances would not be considered existent under the 'beside each other' condition because they are not necessarily adjacent. The author then ties this to the Christian classification (substance, body, accident) to show that if the premise were true, even the Christian conclusion that God is a substance would be invalid.
5
Conclude: both premise and Christian doctrine are false
The author concludes that both the original claim about spatial relation and the Christian substance doctrine are false. Reflect on why this matters for tawhid: Allah is not bound by spatial categories.
The phrase 'والله الموفق' signals the end of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يكون أحدهما بجهة صاحبه أو بجنبه. قلنا: هذا منكم تقسيم للموجودين، وليس من ضرورة الوجود أحد الأمرين، لأنهما إن كانا موجودين، كان أحدهما بجهة صاحبه، ينبغي أن لا يكون الجوهر وما قام به من العرض موجودين، لأن أحدهما ليس بجهة صاحبه. وإن كانا موجودين لأن أحدهما بجنب صاحبه ينبغي أن لا يكون الجوهران موجودين لأن أحدهما ليس بحيث صاحبه، وقد ما بطلان هذا من إبطال قول النصارى أن الموجود إما أن يكون جوهرا، وإما أن يكون جسما، وإما أن يكون عرضا. والباري جل وعلا ليس بجسم ولا عرض. فدل أنه جوهر فإن بطل ذلك بطل هذا. وإن صح هذا صح ذلك. بل كلا الأمرين باطل لما مر، والله الموفق.
One is at the direction of its companion or beside it. We said: This is a division of existents from you, and neither of the two matters is necessary for existence, because if they are both existent, one being at the direction of its companion, then the substance and the accident that inheres in it ought not to be existent, since one is not at the direction of its companion. And if they are both existent because one is beside its companion, then two substances ought not to be existent, since one is not in the place of its companion. And how this falsehood is established from the refutation of the Christians' statement that the existent is either a substance, or a body, or an accident. The Exalted Creator is neither a body nor an accident. So it indicates that He is a substance; then if that is false, this is false. And if this is true, that is true. Rather, both matters are false due to what has passed, and God is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that two existents must be in spatial relation, such as one being at the direction of the other or beside it. The author identifies this as an imposition by the opponent, not a necessary condition of existence. He uses a reductio ad absurdum: if the premise were necessary, then a substance and its accidents would not be considered existent because the accident is not at the direction of the substance. Similarly, two substances would not be considered existent because they are not beside each other in a required manner. The author then ties this to the Christian claim that existents are only substance, body, or accident. Since God is neither body nor accident, Christians conclude He must be substance. The author shows that the original premise leads to absurdity, so both the premise and the Christian conclusion are invalid.

Key Terms
جهة
جوهر
عرض
جسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that for two things to exist, one must be in a spatial relation (direction or adjacency) to the other.
Correction: Existence does not require spatial relation; Allah exists without being in any direction or place, and even created substances and accidents can coexist without such relations (e.g., substance and accident are not at the direction of each other).

Checkpoint
What claim about two existents does the author refute in this passage?
A) They must be similar in essence.
B) They must be in spatial relation (one at the direction of or beside the other).
C) They must be substances.
D) They must be bodies.

Key Takeaway
Two existents do not need to be spatially related; God's existence is independent of direction, place, or category.

The Karrāmi Challenge About Direction and Abu Ishaq's Insufficient Answer
The author recounts a historical question about direction and presents Abu Ishaq's insufficient answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Karrāmi argument that negating direction for God implies non-existence, and why Abu Ishaq's response was insufficient according to the author.

Core Idea
A question unanswered is a proof unbegun.

The Karrāmiyya raised a deep challenge: if God has no direction, how can He exist? Sultan Mahmud passed this question to theologians. Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini attempted an answer, but the author points out that he only gave the start of a proof, not a direct rebuttal. This lesson unpacks the question, the historical episode, and the logical insufficiency of the response.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Karrāmi claim
Read the opening sentence: they claim that non-existence is more certain than negating the six directions for God. Ask yourself why they think that.
The Karrāmi argument is that anything without direction cannot be conceived to exist.
2
Follow the historical chain
Notice the chain: Sultan Mahmud receives the question, passes it to Ibn Fūrak, who writes to Abu Ishaq. Note that Ibn Fūrak did not record his own answer.
This shows the question was considered important and passed to a leading theologian.
3
Analyze Abu Ishaq's response
Read Abu Ishaq's argument: if God had direction, He would be limited, hence divisible and composite. This is impossible. But does it answer the original question? The author says it is only the beginning of a proof, not a rebuttal.
The original question was: why does negating direction not imply non-existence? Abu Ishaq only shows that direction leads to impossibility, which is indirect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما يزعمون أنه لا عدم أشد تحققا من نفي المذكور من الجهات الست. وما لا جهة له لا يتصور وجوده. فنقول: ذكر أبو إسحاق الأسفراييني أن السلطان يعني به السلطان محمود بن سبكتكين قبل هذا السؤال من القوم من الكرامية، وألقاه على ابن فورك، قال: وكتب به ابن فورك إلي، ولم يكتب بماذا أجاب. ثم اشتغل أبو إسحاق بالجواب، ولم يأت بما هو انفصال عن هذا السؤال، بل أتى بما هو ابتداء دليل في المسألة، من أنه لو كان بجهة لكان محدودا، وما جاز عليه التحديد جاز الانقسام والتجزؤ. ولأن ما جاز عليه الجهة جاز عليه الوصف والتركيب، وهو أن يتصل به الأجسام، وذا باطل بالإجماع. ولأنه لو جاز عليه الجهة لجازت إحاطة الأجسام به على نحو ما قررنا، وهذا كله ابتداء الدليل وليس بدفع للسؤال.
And what they claim: that non-existence is more certain than the negation of the mentioned from the six directions. And that which has no direction cannot be conceived to exist. We say: Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini mentioned that the Sultan — meaning Sultan Mahmud bin Sebuktekin — received this question from the Karrāmi group, and he posed it to Ibn Fūrak. He said: Ibn Fūrak wrote to me about it, but he did not write what answer he gave. Then Abu Ishaq busied himself with the answer, but he did not produce a separation from this question; rather he produced what is a beginning of a proof in the issue: that if He had direction, He would be limited, and what is subject to limitation is subject to division and partitioning. And because what is subject to direction is subject to description and composition, which is that bodies attach to Him, and this is invalid by consensus. And because if direction were possible for Him, then bodies surrounding Him would be possible in the manner we established. And all of this is a beginning of the proof, not a rebuttal of the question.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage introduces the Karrāmi claim that non-existence is more certain than negating the six directions for God; in other words, anything without direction cannot be imagined to exist, so if we negate direction for God, we are effectively negating His existence. The author then narrates a historical episode: Sultan Mahmud of Ghazna received this question from the Karrāmiyya and passed it to Ibn Fūrak, who wrote to Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini. Abu Ishaq attempted an answer but did not provide a direct refutation; instead, he gave an initial proof: if God had direction, He would be limited, and whatever is limited is divisible and composed, which is impossible for God. Additionally, direction would imply that bodies could attach to or surround Him, which is invalid by consensus. The author notes that this is only the beginning of a proof, not a proper rebuttal to the original question, because it does not address why the negation of direction does not entail non-existence.

Correction: The author explicitly says it is only the beginning of a proof, not a rebuttal, because it does not address why negating direction does not imply non-existence.

The Karrāmi Objection and Its Resolution
Objection and resolution on divine direction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Karrāmi objection to negating divine direction and the author's logical resolution based on the precise meaning of negation.

Core Idea
Negating direction only negates existence if direction is necessary for existence — a premise not granted.

The Karrāmi sect argued that if you deny that Allah is in a direction, you implicitly deny His existence, because every existent must be somewhere. This objection presents a challenge to those who affirm divine transcendence above physical location. In this passage, the author presents the objection and then provides a precise logical resolution by analyzing what is actually being negated.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: first the Karrāmi sets up a dilemma, then the author responds.
The objection claims the same evidence used to negate direction also threatens to negate existence.
2
Identify the dilemma
The Karrāmi says: your evidence against direction implies that if we don't affirm direction, we would be affirming non-existence. So your evidence cuts both ways. See how this is a problem.
3
Understand the disjunctive analysis
The author asks: does negating all directions entail non-existence of something that IS in a direction or something that is NOT? This is the key move.
There are two possibilities: (a) something that is in a direction, or (b) something that is not in a direction.
4
See how each possibility is refuted
If (a), then why assume Allah is in a direction? If (b), then negating directions has no effect on something that is not in a direction, so existence is unaffected. Thus the objection fails.
The author concludes that the Karrāmi have repeated a previously refuted fallacy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللكرامي أن يقول: إن ما ذكرت من الأدلة يوجب بطلان القول بالجهة، لما في إثباتها من إثبات أمارات الحدوث، فما ذكرت من الدليل يوجب القول بالجهة لما في الامتناع عن القول به إثبات عدمه، وكما لا يجوز إثبات حدوث ما ثبت قدمه بالدليل، لا يجوز نفي ما ثبت وجوده بالدليل. وحل هذا الإشكال أن يقال: إن النفي عن الجهات كلها يوجب عدم ما هو بجهة من النافي أو عدم ما ليس بجهة منه؟ فإن قال: عدم ما هو بجهة منه. قلنا: نعم. ولكن لم قلتم أن الباري بجهة من النافي؟ فإن قال: لأنه لو لم يكن بجهة منه لكان معدوما فقد عاد إلى ما تقدم من الشبهة، وقد فرغنا بحمد الله عن حلها. وإن قال: النفي عن الجهات يوجب عدم ما ليس بجهة منه. فقد أحال، لأن ذاك لا عدم النافي، وما قام به من الأعراض لما لم يكن بجهة من نفسه فكذا لا يوجب عدم الباري لأنه ليس بجهة من النافي.
And the Karrāmi may say: Indeed, what you mentioned of evidences necessitates the invalidity of the statement about direction, because affirming it entails affirming signs of temporality. Then what you mentioned of the evidence necessitates the statement about direction, because refraining from stating it entails affirming His non-existence. And just as it is not permissible to affirm temporality for what has been proven eternal by evidence, it is not permissible to negate what has been proven existent by evidence. And the resolution of this difficulty is to say: The negation of all directions entails the non-existence of that which is in a direction from the one negating, or the non-existence of that which is not in a direction from him? If he says: the non-existence of that which is in a direction from him. We say: yes. But why do you say that the Creator is in a direction from the one negating? If he says: because if He were not in a direction from him, He would be non-existent—then he has returned to the previous fallacy, and we have finished resolving it by God's grace. And if he says: the negation of directions entails the non-existence of that which is not in a direction from him—then he has made an impossible statement, because that (i.e., something not in a direction) does not entail the non-existence of the one negating, and the accidents inhering in it, since they are not in a direction from themselves, so likewise they do not entail the non-existence of the Creator because He is not in a direction from the one negating.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage presents the Karrāmi objection to Abu Ishaq's argument and then provides the author's definitive resolution. The Karrāmi argues: Your evidence against direction implies that affirming direction leads to signs of temporality, so direction is invalid. But your same evidence also implies that if we refrain from affirming direction, we would be affirming God's non-existence, because something without direction cannot exist. Thus, your own proof cuts both ways: it cannot both negate direction and leave existence intact. The author resolves this by analyzing the meaning of negating all directions. He asks: Does negating all directions entail the non-existence of (a) something that is actually in a direction (from the perspective of the one negating), or (b) something that is not in a direction? If the opponent says (a), then we ask why he assumes God is in a direction. If he answers that without direction God would be non-existent, then he is repeating the same fallacy already refuted. If he says (b), then that is self-contradictory because if something is not in a direction, negating directions has no impact on its existence, just as accidents do not require direction for their existence. Thus, the objection fails.

Key Terms
جهة
حدوث
قدم
نفي
إثبات
شبهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something has no direction, it cannot exist.
Correction: Existence does not require direction; accidents exist without having a direction themselves. Negating direction only threatens existence if direction is a necessary condition, which is not proven.

Checkpoint
What is the core of the Karrāmi objection presented in this passage?
A) That direction is a necessary attribute of God.
B) That the same evidence used to negate direction could also be used to negate God's existence.
C) That God's existence is proven by consensus.
D) That direction is impossible for any existent.

Key Takeaway
The Karrāmi objection fails because it equivocates on what it means to negate direction; negating a non-essential attribute does not negate existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's existence, have you ever imposed mental conditions like location or space that are not necessary? How does that limit your understanding of His transcendence?
?
The Karrāmi felt compelled to assert direction for God because they could not conceive existence without it. Where in your life do you mistake your own conceptual limits for divine reality?
?

The First Horn: Assuming God in a Direction
The author presents the first horn of the dilemma and refutes it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the Karrami objection by exposing its assumption that God must be in a direction.

Core Idea
Negating direction only threatens existence if the entity is assumed to be in a direction.

The Karramis argued that if Allah has no direction, He must be non-existent. The author begins his resolution by forcing the opponent to clarify what exactly negation of directions eliminates. In this first part, he tackles the claim that negation leads to non-existence of what is in a direction — and shows it begs the question.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noticing the structure of the dilemma presented.
The opponent is forced to choose between two options.
2
Identify the first option
The opponent says negation of directions leads to non-existence of what is in a direction. The author agrees but asks: why do you assume God is in a direction?
This is the key move.
3
Understand the logical implication
If the opponent's assumption is unwarranted, the argument collapses. The author points out that this is a return to a previously resolved doubt.
The author refers to an earlier resolution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحل هذا الإشكال أن يقال: إن النفي عن الجهات كلها يوجب عدم ما هو بجهة من النافي أو عدم ما ليس بجهة منه ؟ فإن قال : عدم ما هو بجهة منه. قلنا: نعم. ولكن لم قلتم أن الباري بجهة من النافي؟ فإن قال : لأنه لو لم يكن بجهة منه لكان معدوما فقد عاد إلى ما تقدم من الشبهة، وقد فرغنا بحمد الله عن حلها. وإن قال: النفي عن الجهات يوجب عدم ما ليس بجهة منه. فقد أحال، لأن ذاك لا عدم النافي، وما قام به من الأعراض لما لم يكن بجهة من نفسه فكذا لا يوجب عدم الباري لأنه ليس بجهة من النافي. فإن قالوا: إذا لم يكن بجهة منه ولا قائما به يكون معدوما. فقد عادوا إلى الشبهة الثالثة، وقد فرغنا عن حلها بتوفيق الله تعالى.
And the resolution of this difficulty is that it is said: Does the negation of all directions necessitate the non-existence of what is in a direction from the one negating, or the non-existence of what is not in a direction from him? If he says: The non-existence of what is in a direction from him. We say: Yes. But why did you say that the Creator (al-Bāri’) is in a direction from the one negating? If he says: Because if He were not in a direction from him, He would be non-existent, then he has returned to the previous doubt, and we have finished resolving it, praise be to God. And if he says: The negation of all directions necessitates the non-existence of what is not in a direction from him. Then he has made an impossibility, because that which is not (the one negating) does not necessitate the non-existence of the one negating, and what subsists in him of accidents, since it is not in a direction from itself, so likewise it does not necessitate the non-existence of the Creator because He is not in a direction from the one negating. And if they say: If He is not in a direction from him nor subsisting in him, He is non-existent. Then they have returned to the third doubt, and we have finished resolving it by the success of God Almighty.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage offers the author's resolution (ḥall) to the Karrami's objection. The author presents a dilemma: the negation of all directions either negates something that is in a direction (from the perspective of the one negating) or negates something that is not in a direction. If the opponent claims that the negation results in non-existence of what is in a direction, then the author points out that this only applies if God is assumed to be in a direction — but that is precisely what is in dispute. If the opponent says that negation results in non-existence of what is not in a direction, then this is absurd because non-existence of a non-directional being does not follow from negation of direction. The author gives an analogy: one's own self (nafsī) and its accidents are not in a direction from oneself, yet they exist. Similarly, God's existence is not contingent on being in a direction. Furthermore, the author notes that if the opponent insists that lack of direction and lack of inherence (qiyām) implies non-existence, that is just a restatement of the third doubt (al-shubha al-thālitha) already resolved earlier in the text.

باري
شبهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if Allah has no direction, He must be non-existent.
Correction: This only follows if Allah is assumed to be essentially in a direction, which is not the case.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main point when the opponent claims negation of directions leads to non-existence of what is in a direction?
A) He agrees and accepts the argument.
B) He agrees but asks why God is assumed to be in a direction.
C) He disagrees completely.
D) He says it is a third doubt.

Key Takeaway
The first horn of the objection fails because it assumes what needs to be proven: that Allah is in a direction.

The opponent here repeated the same doubt because he couldn't think beyond his initial assumption. In your own understanding of Allah, are there areas where you keep circling back to the same questions without allowing the deeper resolution to settle in your heart? What is one assumption about Allah that you have never truly questioned?
?

The Second Horn: Negating What is Not in a Direction
The author refutes the second horn and dismisses it as absurd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author considers the second horn of the Karrami objection impossible and how he uses the analogy of the self and its accidents.

Core Idea
Negating direction from a non-directional being does not cause its non-existence.

The Karrami opponent might argue that negating all directions from God would eliminate something that is not in a direction — meaning God is non-existent. The author refutes this by showing that non-existence does not follow from the negation of direction. He gives a profound analogy: your own self and its accidents are not in a direction from you, yet they exist.

Guided Path
1
Read the second horn
Read the second part of the passage, from 'وإن قال' to the end. Notice the analogy.
2
Understand the impossibility
The author says this option is impossible (فقد أحال). The reasoning: that which is not in a direction does not necessitate non-existence of the one negating.
The author uses the example of one's own self and accidents.
3
Connect to earlier doubts
If the opponent insists that lack of direction and lack of inherence implies non-existence, that is just the third doubt already resolved.
The author refers back to a previous resolution.

Existence Does Not Require Direction or Subsistence
Nasafi refutes the claim that existence entails direction or subsistence using the soul example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why direction and subsistence are not necessary concomitants of existence, and how Nasafi uses the example of the soul to refute the objection that God would be non-existent without them.

Core Idea
Direction and subsistence are not necessities of existence; even the soul exists without being in a direction.

Nasafi confronts a logical objection: if God is not in a direction and not subsistent in something, then He would be non-existent. To refute this, he argues that direction and subsistence are not necessary properties of existence at all. He provides a compelling example from our own experience: the soul and the accidents that inhere in it exist without being in any direction relative to us. This lesson walks through that argument carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal English translation. Notice the flow of the argument from objection to response to example.
Pay attention to the terms 'direction' (جهة) and 'subsistent' (قائم).
2
Identify the objection and Nasafi's response
The passage starts with an objection: 'If it is not in a direction nor subsistent in it, it would be non-existent.' Nasafi refutes by first affirming God's existence from undeniable proofs, then showing that direction implies temporal origination, which is impossible for the Eternal.
This sets the stage for the core argument.
3
Examine the soul example
Nasafi uses the soul (nafs) and the accidents (a'rād) that subsist in it as an example: these exist but are not in a direction relative to the self. Conversely, objects in a direction (e.g., on your right) are not subsistent in you. Thus direction and subsistence are not necessary for existence.
The example covers all six directions.
4
Understand the conclusion
Since direction and subsistence are not necessary concomitants (lawaḥiq) of existence in created things, they are even less applicable to the Creator. This argument supports the negation of God being an accident, substance, or body.
The final sentence ties the argument to broader theological negations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: إذا لم يكن بجهة منه ولا قائما به يكون معدوما. فقد عادوا إلى الشبهة الثالثة، وقد فرغا عن حلها بتوفيق الله تعالى والأصل في هذا كله أن ثبوت الصانع جل وعلا وقدمه علم بما لا مدفع له من الدلائل ولا مجال للريب فيه، فقلنا بثبوته وقدمه، وعرفنا استحالة ثبوت أمارات الحدوث في القديم، فنفينا ذلك عنه لما في إثباتها من إثبات حدوث القديم أو بطلان دلائل الحدوث وذلك باطل كله على ما قررنا وفي إثبات المكان والجهة إثبات دلالة الحدوث على ما مر، وليس من ضرورة الوجود إثبات الجهة، لأن نفسي وما قام بها من الأعراض ليست مني بجهة وهي موجودة، وما كان مني بجهة ليس بقائم بي وهو موجود وكذا ليس من ضرورة الوجود أن يكون فوقي، لوجود ما ليس فوقي ولا أن يكون تحتي، لوجود ما ليس تحتي، وكذا قدامي وخلفي وعن يميني وعن يساري، وإذا ثبت هذا في كل جهة على اليقين ثبت في الجهات كلها، إذ هي متركبة من الأفراد. فإذا ليس من ضرورة الوجود أن يكون مني بجهة لوجود ما ليس مني بجهة، ولا أن يكون بي، لوجود ما ليس بقائم بي. وظهر أن قيام الشيء بي وكونه بجهة مني ليس من لواحق الوجود وضروراته على ما قررنا هذا الكلام في نفي كونه تعالى عرضا أو جوهرا أو جسما
For they said: If it is not in a direction from it nor subsistent in it, it would be non-existent. So they have returned to the third doubt, and we have already finished solving it with the success of Allah the Exalted. The principle in all this is that the existence of the Maker, Exalted and High, and His pre-eternity are known by proofs that are undeniable and of no room for doubt. So we affirm His existence and His pre-eternity, and we know the impossibility of establishing signs of temporal origination in the pre-eternal. So we negated that from Him because affirming them would entail affirming the temporal origination of the pre-eternal or invalidating the proofs of temporal origination, and all that is false as we established. And in affirming place and direction there is affirming an indication of temporal origination as has passed. And it is not a necessity of existence to affirm direction, because my soul and the accidents that subsist in it are not from me in a direction, yet they exist; and what is from me in a direction is not subsistent in me, yet it exists. Likewise, it is not a necessity of existence that it be above me, because there exists what is not above me; nor that it be below me, because there exists what is not below me; likewise in front of me, behind me, to my right and to my left. And if this is established concerning each individual direction with certainty, it is established concerning all directions, since they are composed of individuals. So it is not a necessity of existence that it be from me in a direction, because there exists what is not from me in a direction; nor that it be in me (subsistent in me), because there exists what is not subsistent in me. And it has become clear that the subsistence of a thing in me and its being in a direction from me are not necessary concomitants and necessities of existence, as we have established. This speech is in negation of His being an accident, or a substance, or a body.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection that if God is not in a direction (jiha) nor subsistent (qā'im) in something, then He would be non-existent. The author (Nasafi) first recalls that the existence and pre-eternity (qidam) of the Maker are established by undeniable proofs, so negating direction does not imply non-existence. He then argues that affirming place or direction for God would imply temporal origination (huduth), which is impossible. The core proof: my soul (nafs) and the accidents (a'rād) that subsist in it are not in a direction relative to me, yet they exist. Conversely, things that are in a direction from me (e.g., a book on my right) are not subsistent in me, yet they exist. Thus direction and subsistence (qiyām) are not necessary for existence. He extends this to all six directions, concluding that direction is not a necessary concomitant (lāhiq) of existence. This argument supports the negation of God being an accident, substance, or body.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: Nasafi demonstrates that the soul and its accidents exist without being in a direction, proving that direction and subsistence are not necessary for existence. Thus, God's existence does not require these attributes.

Checkpoint
What does Nasafi use as an example to prove that existence does not require being in a direction?
A) A body and its parts
B) The soul and its accidents
C) Color and its substrate
D) Time and space

Key Takeaway
Existence alone does not require being in a direction or being subsistent in something; God's existence is beyond such limitations.

Distinguishing the Rational from the Imaginary
Nasafi distinguishes rational conceivability from imaginability and argues the latter is not a criterion for existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an existent can be rationally conceivable but not imaginable, and why lack of imagination does not imply non-existence.

Core Idea
Reason can affirm what imagination cannot picture.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi establishes a foundational distinction: rational conceivability (ma'qul) is broader than imagination (mawhum). He argues that just because something cannot be pictured in the mind does not mean it does not exist. This is critical for understanding how we can know Allah, who is beyond sensory perception.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening statement
Nasafi states that an existent free from accidents and body is rationally conceivable. Note the phrase 'not a necessity of the existent to affirm any of these meanings.'
This sets up the scope of rational conceivability.
2
Contrast with imagination
He then says it is not imaginable because no such being has been sensed. Why is this distinction necessary?
Imagination is tied to sensory experience.
3
Understand the key conclusion
Lack of imagination does not entail non-existence. Rational proofs establish the distinction.
This is the main takeaway.
4
Reflect on the theological importance
This distinction is essential for affirming God's existence without restricting Him to what can be imagined.
God is beyond imagination, but known through reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخرج الموجود عن هذه المعاني كلها معقول لما بينا من الدلائل، أن ليس من ضرورة الموجود ثبوت معنى من هذه المعاني، لما مر من ثبوت موجود ليس فيه كل معنى من هذه المعاني على اليقين. غير أنه ليس بموهوم لما لم يحس موجود يعرى عن هذه المعاني كلها. إذ ما يشاهد من المحسوسات كلها محدثة، وارتفاع دلالة الحدوث عن المحدث محال، وفي الغائب الأمر بخلافه، وليس من ضرورة الارتفاع عن الوهم العدم، لما ثبت من الدلائل العقلية على الحدوث وظهر التفرقة بين المعقول والموهوم على ما تقدم ذكره على وجه لا يبقى للمنصف فيه ريبة ثم إن الله تعالى أثبت في نفس كل عاقل معاني خارجة عن الوهم لخروجها عن درك الحواس، ويعلم وجودها على وجه لم يكن للشك فيه مدخل لثبوت آثارها كالعقل والروح، والبصر والسمع والشم والذوق، فإن ثبوت هذه المعاني متحقق، والأفهام عن الإحاطة بما بينها قاصرة لخروجها عن الحواس المؤدية المدركة صور محسوساتها إلى الفكرة، ليصير ذلك حجة على كل من أنكر الصانع مع ظهور الآيات الدلالة عليه لخروجه عن التصور في الوهم، ويعلم أن لا مدخل للوهم في معرفة ثبوت الأشياء الغائبة عن الحواس، ومن أراد الوصول إلى ذلك بالوهم ونفي ما لم يتصور فيه مع ظهور آيات ثبوته، فقد عطل الدليل القائم، لانعدام ما ليس يصلح دليلا، فيصير كمن أنكر وجود البياض في جسم معاينته ذلك، لانعدام استدراك ذلك بالسمع، وجهالة من هذا فعله لا يخفي على الناس فكذا هذا. ثم لا فرق بين من أنكر الشيء لخروجه عن الوهم، وبين من جعل خروج الشيء عن الوهم دليلا للعدم لما فيهما جميعا قصر ثبوت الشيء ووجوده على الوهم، وخروج الموجود عن جميع أمارات الحدوث غير موهوم لما لم يعاين موجودا ليس بمحدث، وإثبات أمارات الحدوث فى القديم محال، وبنفيها عن القديم إخراجه عن الوهم وبخروجه عن الوهم يلتحق بالعدم فإذا لا وجود للقديم. فصارت المجسمة والقائلون بالجهة الجاعلون ما لا يجوز عليه الجهة في حيز
And the existent being free from all these meanings is rationally conceivable by the proofs we have shown, that it is not a necessity of the existent to affirm any of these meanings, because it has passed the affirmation of an existent that does not contain any of these meanings with certainty. However, it is not imaginable because no sensible existent that is devoid of all these meanings has been sensed. Since what is observed of sensible things are all temporal originated, and raising the indication of temporal origination from the originated is impossible, but in the unseen the matter is contrary. And it is not a necessity that the lack of imagination entails non-existence, because of what has been established from rational proofs of temporal origination, and the distinction between the rational and the imaginary has become clear as previously mentioned in a manner that leaves no doubt for the unbiased. Then Allah the Exalted has established in the mind of every rational being meanings external to the imagination because they are beyond the perception of the senses, and their existence is known in a way that allows no entry for doubt due to the establishment of their effects, such as reason and the soul, and sight, hearing, smell, taste. For the existence of these meanings is certain, and the intellects are incapable of comprehending what is between them because they are beyond the senses that convey and perceive the forms of their sensibles to thought. So that becomes an argument against everyone who denies the Maker despite the appearance of the signs indicating Him, because He is beyond conceptualization in the imagination. And it is known that the imagination has no entry in knowing the existence of things absent from the senses. And whoever seeks to attain that by imagination and negates what is not conceptualized in it despite the appearance of signs of its existence has suspended the established proof, due to the lack of what is not a valid proof. So he becomes like one who denies the existence of whiteness in a body while observing it, because of the lack of perceiving that by hearing. The ignorance of such a person is not hidden from people. So likewise this. Then there is no difference between one who denies a thing because it is beyond imagination, and one who makes the fact that a thing is beyond imagination a proof of its non-existence, because both confine the existence and reality of things to imagination. And the existent being free from all signs of temporal origination is not imaginable because no existent that is not temporal originated has been observed. Affirming signs of temporal origination in the pre-eternal is impossible, and by negating them from the pre-eternal, it is removed from imagination, and by its removal from imagination it joins non-existence. Thus the pre-eternal would have no existence. So the corpusporealists and those who affirm direction, who place what does not admit of direction in a place...
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage is a two-part argument. First, it asserts that an existent free from accident, substance, and body is rationally conceivable (ma'qul), but not imaginable (mawhum) because no such being is sensed. The lack of imagination does not imply non-existence, as rational proofs establish temporal origination and distinguish the rational from the imaginary. Second, the author provides evidence from human experience: Allah has placed in every rational being concepts beyond imagination, such as reason ('aql), soul (rūh), and the internal senses (sight, hearing, smell, taste), which exist but are not perceived by the external senses. Their existence is known through their effects. This is used as an analogy for God, who is beyond sensory perception and imagination. The author then criticizes two types of error: (1) denying something because it cannot be imagined, and (2) using the fact that something is beyond imagination as a proof of its non-existence. Both errors confine existence to the imaginable. This leads to a problem: if signs of temporality are negated from God, He becomes unimaginable, and if one equates unimaginability with non-existence, then God would be non-existent. The passage concludes by identifying the mujassima (anthropomorphists) and those who affirm direction (jiha) for God as falling into this error, as they place God in a location (hayyiz), which is impossible.

Key Terms

موهوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Believing that only what can be imagined truly exists.
Correction: This passage shows that rational conceivability is a broader category than imagination, and lack of imagination does not equal non-existence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between rational conceivability (ma'qul) and imagination (mawhum)?
A) They are identical.
B) The rational is always imaginable.
C) The rational can be beyond imagination.
D) Imagination is a prerequisite for rationality.

Key Takeaway
Rational conceivability is independent of imagination; not everything that exists can be imagined.

?
When you worship, do you find yourself trying to visualize God? How can you train your heart to focus on the rational understanding of His existence without reducing Him to an image? Commit to a specific practice that strengthens this.

Proving Unseen Realities Through Their Effects
Nasafi lists unseen realities known through effects and uses them as an analogy for knowing God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Allah has established knowledge of unseen realities like reason and the soul through their observable effects, and how this serves as an analogy for knowing God.

Core Idea
Effects prove existence even when the cause is beyond senses.

Nasafi provides concrete examples from human experience to demonstrate that things can exist without being imaginable or perceptible. Reason, soul, and the internal senses are known through their effects, even though they themselves are not sensed. This is used as a powerful analogy for knowing Allah, who is known through His signs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the examples given
Read the passage starting with 'ثم إن الله تعالى أثبت' and list the unseen realities Nasafi mentions: reason, soul, sight, hearing, smell, taste.
These are known through their effects, not by direct perception.
2
Understand how their existence is known
Their existence is certain because of their manifest effects, even though they are beyond sensory perception.
This is the key argument: effects prove the cause.
3
Apply the analogy to God
Just as these unseen realities are known through effects, God is known through His creation (signs).
This counters those who deny God because they cannot imagine Him.
4
Reflect on the critique
Nasafi criticizes those who deny a thing because it cannot be imagined, likening them to someone denying whiteness in a body because they cannot hear it.
This illustrates the absurdity of limiting existence to what can be imagined.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخرج الموجود عن هذه المعاني كلها معقول لما بينا من الدلائل، أن ليس من ضرورة الموجود ثبوت معنى من هذه المعاني، لما مر من ثبوت موجود ليس فيه كل معنى من هذه المعاني على اليقين. غير أنه ليس بموهوم لما لم يحس موجود يعرى عن هذه المعاني كلها. إذ ما يشاهد من المحسوسات كلها محدثة، وارتفاع دلالة الحدوث عن المحدث محال، وفي الغائب الأمر بخلافه، وليس من ضرورة الارتفاع عن الوهم العدم، لما ثبت من الدلائل العقلية على الحدوث وظهر التفرقة بين المعقول والموهوم على ما تقدم ذكره على وجه لا يبقى للمنصف فيه ريبة ثم إن الله تعالى أثبت في نفس كل عاقل معاني خارجة عن الوهم لخروجها عن درك الحواس، ويعلم وجودها على وجه لم يكن للشك فيه مدخل لثبوت آثارها كالعقل والروح، والبصر والسمع والشم والذوق، فإن ثبوت هذه المعاني متحقق، والأفهام عن الإحاطة بما بينها قاصرة لخروجها عن الحواس المؤدية المدركة صور محسوساتها إلى الفكرة، ليصير ذلك حجة على كل من أنكر الصانع مع ظهور الآيات الدلالة عليه لخروجه عن التصور في الوهم، ويعلم أن لا مدخل للوهم في معرفة ثبوت الأشياء الغائبة عن الحواس، ومن أراد الوصول إلى ذلك بالوهم ونفي ما لم يتصور فيه مع ظهور آيات ثبوته، فقد عطل الدليل القائم، لانعدام ما ليس يصلح دليلا، فيصير كمن أنكر وجود البياض في جسم معاينته ذلك، لانعدام استدراك ذلك بالسمع، وجهالة من هذا فعله لا يخفي على الناس فكذا هذا. ثم لا فرق بين من أنكر الشيء لخروجه عن الوهم، وبين من جعل خروج الشيء عن الوهم دليلا للعدم لما فيهما جميعا قصر ثبوت الشيء ووجوده على الوهم، وخروج الموجود عن جميع أمارات الحدوث غير موهوم لما لم يعاين موجودا ليس بمحدث، وإثبات أمارات الحدوث فى القديم محال، وبنفيها عن القديم إخراجه عن الوهم وبخروجه عن الوهم يلتحق بالعدم فإذا لا وجود للقديم. فصارت المجسمة والقائلون بالجهة الجاعلون ما لا يجوز عليه الجهة في حيز
And the existent being free from all these meanings is rationally conceivable by the proofs we have shown, that it is not a necessity of the existent to affirm any of these meanings, because it has passed the affirmation of an existent that does not contain any of these meanings with certainty. However, it is not imaginable because no sensible existent that is devoid of all these meanings has been sensed. Since what is observed of sensible things are all temporal originated, and raising the indication of temporal origination from the originated is impossible, but in the unseen the matter is contrary. And it is not a necessity that the lack of imagination entails non-existence, because of what has been established from rational proofs of temporal origination, and the distinction between the rational and the imaginary has become clear as previously mentioned in a manner that leaves no doubt for the unbiased. Then Allah the Exalted has established in the mind of every rational being meanings external to the imagination because they are beyond the perception of the senses, and their existence is known in a way that allows no entry for doubt due to the establishment of their effects, such as reason and the soul, and sight, hearing, smell, taste. For the existence of these meanings is certain, and the intellects are incapable of comprehending what is between them because they are beyond the senses that convey and perceive the forms of their sensibles to thought. So that becomes an argument against everyone who denies the Maker despite the appearance of the signs indicating Him, because He is beyond conceptualization in the imagination. And it is known that the imagination has no entry in knowing the existence of things absent from the senses. And whoever seeks to attain that by imagination and negates what is not conceptualized in it despite the appearance of signs of its existence has suspended the established proof, due to the lack of what is not a valid proof. So he becomes like one who denies the existence of whiteness in a body while observing it, because of the lack of perceiving that by hearing. The ignorance of such a person is not hidden from people. So likewise this. Then there is no difference between one who denies a thing because it is beyond imagination, and one who makes the fact that a thing is beyond imagination a proof of its non-existence, because both confine the existence and reality of things to imagination. And the existent being free from all signs of temporal origination is not imaginable because no existent that is not temporal originated has been observed. Affirming signs of temporal origination in the pre-eternal is impossible, and by negating them from the pre-eternal, it is removed from imagination, and by its removal from imagination it joins non-existence. Thus the pre-eternal would have no existence. So the corpusporealists and those who affirm direction, who place what does not admit of direction in a place...
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage is a two-part argument. First, it asserts that an existent free from accident, substance, and body is rationally conceivable (ma'qul), but not imaginable (mawhum) because no such being is sensed. The lack of imagination does not imply non-existence, as rational proofs establish temporal origination and distinguish the rational from the imaginary. Second, the author provides evidence from human experience: Allah has placed in every rational being concepts beyond imagination, such as reason ('aql), soul (rūh), and the internal senses (sight, hearing, smell, taste), which exist but are not perceived by the external senses. Their existence is known through their effects. This is used as an analogy for God, who is beyond sensory perception and imagination. The author then criticizes two types of error: (1) denying something because it cannot be imagined, and (2) using the fact that something is beyond imagination as a proof of its non-existence. Both errors confine existence to the imaginable. This leads to a problem: if signs of temporality are negated from God, He becomes unimaginable, and if one equates unimaginability with non-existence, then God would be non-existent. The passage concludes by identifying the mujassima (anthropomorphists) and those who affirm direction (jiha) for God as falling into this error, as they place God in a location (hayyiz), which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What excuse will you give on the Day when you stand before the One whose signs were all around you, but you demanded to see Him first?

If you have been ignoring these signs, what concrete step will you take tonight to acknowledge Him as the Maker behind the effects you witness?

The Error of Confining Existence to Imagination
Nasafi critiques the error of equating imagination with existence and identifies anthropomorphists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why equating imagination with existence leads to the denial of the pre-eternal (God) and understand the critique of the anthropomorphists (mujassima).

Core Idea
Limiting existence to imagination destroys the proof for God's existence.

Nasafi now exposes a dangerous error: if one only accepts what can be imagined, then God, who has no signs of temporal origination, becomes unimaginable, leading to existential non-existence. He identifies the mujassima (anthropomorphists) and those who affirm direction for God as falling into this error by confining God to a location.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage identifying two closely related errors
Nasafi states there is no difference between one who denies a thing because it is beyond imagination, and one who uses its being beyond imagination as proof of its non-existence.
Both errors confine existence to the imaginable.
2
Understand the consequence for God
An existent free from all signs of temporal origination (i.e., God) is not imaginable. If unimaginability implies non-existence, then God would be non-existent.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Identify the groups criticized
Nasafi mentions the mujassima (anthropomorphists) and those who affirm direction (jiha) for God, as they place God in a location (hayyiz), which is impossible.
They commit the error of confining God to what can be imagined.

Natural Disposition vs. Rational Proof: Responding to the Claim of Innate Belief in God's Direction
Al-Nasafi answers the objection that people naturally believe God is above.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nasafi refutes the objection that innate human disposition proves God's direction, by distinguishing between ignorant whim and scholarly proof.

Core Idea
Not all natural beliefs are true; scholarly proof overrides raw disposition.

Al-Nasafi tackles a common objection: 'People are naturally inclined to believe God is above, so it must be true.' In this passage, he dissects this claim by asking who 'people' refers to. For the untrained masses, it may be true—but only because they follow whim and ignorance. For the learned scholar, it is false, because rational evidence proves God is beyond direction. This lesson shows how he uses this distinction to defend divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how al-Nasafi first states the objection before giving his answer.
The objection claims innate knowledge; the response challenges its universality.
2
Identify the two interpretations of 'people'
Find where al-Nasafi splits 'people' into two groups: the ignorant commoner and the skilled scholar. Note the conditions he sets for each.
This is the key move in his argument.
3
Understand why the commoner believes in direction
Examine al-Nasafi's explanation: the commoner lacks rational skill, associates direction with perfection, and follows imagination (wahm).
He gives three reasons: ignorance of signs of origination, seeing objects in direction, and flawed reasoning.
4
Understand why the scholar rejects direction
Contrast with the scholar: he knows the categories of possible/impossible and relies on proof (dalīl), which shows God cannot be in a direction.
Proof trumps imagination.
5
Reflect on the lesson's takeaway
Ask yourself: how does this argument protect divine transcendence? What does it teach about the role of reason in theology?
The passage defends tanzih (transcendence) and prioritizes rational proof over raw disposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا هو الجواب عن قولهم: إن الناس مجبولون على العلم أنه تعالى في جهة العلو، حتى إنهم لو تركوا وما هم عليه مجبولون لاعتقدوا أن صانعهم في جهة العلو. فإنا نقول لهم: إن عنيتم بهذا من لم يرض عقله بالتدبير والتفكير ولم يتمهر في معرفة الحقائق بإدمان النظر والتأمل فمسلم أنه بهواه يعتقد أن صانعه بجهة منه، لما أنه لا يعرف أن التجيز بجهة من أمارات الحدوث وهي منفية عن القديم ولما يرى أن ما ليس بقائم به يكون بجهة، ثم يرى صفاء الأجرام العلوية وشرف الأجسام المنيرة في الحسن، فظن جهلا منه أنه تعالى لابد من كونه بتلك الجهة منه، لخروج ما ليس بقائم به ولا بجهة منه عن الوهم وفضيلة تلك الجهة على سائر الجهات عنده، وإن عنيتم به الحذاق من العلماء العارفين بالفرق بين الجائز والممتنع والممكن والمحال فغير مسلم، إذ هؤلاء يثبتون الأمر على الدليل دون الوهم وقد قام الدليل عندهم على استحالة كونه تعالى في جهة. والله الموفق.
And this is the answer to their statement: 'People are naturally disposed (majbūlūn) to know that He, exalted, is in the direction of above, such that if they were left to their natural disposition they would believe that their Creator is in the direction of above.' So we say to them: If you mean by this one who does not satisfy his intellect with reflection and contemplation, and has not become skilled in knowing truths by persistent investigation and meditation, then it is granted that he, by his whim, believes that his Creator is in a direction, because he does not know that occupying a direction is among the signs of origination (amārāt al-ḥudūth), which are negated from the Eternal (al-qadīm); and because he sees that what is not self-subsistent is in a direction, then he sees the purity of the celestial bodies and the honor of the luminous bodies in beauty, so he ignorantly thinks that the Exalted must necessarily be in that direction, because what is not self-subsistent and not in a direction is outside his imagination, and because of the excellence of that direction over other directions in his view. But if you mean by it the skillful scholars who know the distinction between the possible (al-jāʾiz) and the impossible (al-mumtaniʿ), the contingent (al-mumkin) and the absurd (al-muḥāl), then it is not granted, for these establish matters on the basis of proof (dalīl) rather than imagination, and proof has been established for them that it is impossible for the Exalted to be in a direction. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage is an answer to the objection that people are naturally inclined to believe that God is in the direction of above. The author distinguishes two interpretations of 'people'. If the objectors mean the common person who does not use reason and critical thought, then it is conceded that such a person, following his whim, believes God is above—due to ignorance and a mistaken association of direction with perfection. But if they mean the class of learned scholars who understand the categories of possibility and impossibility, then the claim is rejected, because these scholars rely on rational proofs which demonstrate the impossibility of God being in a direction. Thus, the natural disposition of the ignorant is not a valid proof.

Key Terms
الجِبِلَّة (al-jibilla)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a commonly held belief, such as God being above, must be true because it is innate or natural.
Correction: The passage shows that the belief of the common person is based on whim and ignorance, not on sound reasoning. True knowledge comes from rational proof, which in this case establishes that God cannot be in a direction.

Checkpoint

C) He rejects the objection entirely by claiming people are not naturally disposed that way.
D) He argues that direction is not a sign of origination.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi defends divine transcendence by showing that the natural belief in God's direction is only true for the ignorant, not for scholars who rely on rational proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Al-Nasafi says that the common person believes God is above because they are not skilled in distinguishing the possible from the impossible. Which belief might that be for you, and what proof would you need to verify it?
?
The scholars in this passage 'establish matters on the basis of proof rather than imagination.' What is one area of your faith where you rely more on cultural upbringing or personal feelings than on sound proof? What concrete step can you take today to replace that imagination with genuine knowledge?
