Divine Transcendence
intermediate
5 min
Refuting the Ash'ari Objection on 'Istila' and the Linguistic Principle
Nasafi refutes the Ash'ari objection and explains the linguistic methodology for divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Nasafi refutes the Ash'ari objection that 'istila' implies prior weakness, and how he establishes a linguistic principle for understanding divine attributes.

Core Idea
The word 'istila' does not linguistically imply weakness; the temporal sequence is customary, not lexical.

The Ash'aris raised a strong objection: if 'istiwa' means 'istila' (mastery), then it would imply that Allah was previously weak before gaining mastery, which is impossible. Nasafi responds with a sophisticated linguistic argument. He shows that the word itself does not carry the meaning of prior weakness; rather, in human experience, mastery often follows weakness as a customary pattern, not as part of the word's definition. He then generalizes this into a principle that applies to other attributes of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Ash'ari objection
The Ash'aris claim that 'istila' in human language comes after a state of weakness. Since Allah never has weakness, the interpretation is invalid.
This is a common argument against metaphorical interpretations.
2
See Nasafi's response on linguistic custom
Nasafi argues that the connection between 'istila' and prior weakness is not based on the word's meaning but on the customary circumstances in the created world. The word itself is neutral on that point.
He gives examples like 'knowledge' and 'power' which in humans come after ignorance and incapacity, but when attributed to Allah they mean perfect attributes without any prior deficiency.
3
Learn the linguistic principle
He formulates a rule: when a word can have two meanings, one possible for Allah and one impossible, the possible meaning is understood when attributed to Allah. He illustrates this with the word 'ajab' (wonder).
This principle prevents anthropomorphism without needing explicit figurative markers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اسْتَوَيْتَ أَنتَ وَمَن مَّعَكَ وَهَذَا النَّوْعُ يَنْقَسِمُ إِلَى قَسِيمٍ يُذْكَرُ وَيُرَادُ بِهِ الْعُلُوُّ مِنْ حَيْثُ الْمَكَانِ وَيُذْكَرُ وَيُرَادُ بِهِ الْعُلُوُّ مِنْ حَيْثُ الرُّتْبَةِ. فَعَلَى هَذَا فَيَحْتَمِلُ أَنْ يَكُونَ الْمُرَادُ مِنْهُ اسْتَوْلَى عَلَى الْعَرْشِ الَّذِي هُوَ أَعْظَمُ الْمَخْلُوقَاتِ وَتَخْصِيصُهُ بِالذِّكْرِ كَانَ تَشْرِيفًا لَهُ إِذْ إِضَافَةُ جُزْئِيَّةِ الْأَشْيَاءِ إِلَى اللَّهِ تَعَالَى لِيَتَعَظَّمَ ذَلِكَ كَمَا قَالَ: {نَاقَةَ اللَّهِ} وَ{أَنَّ الْمَسَاجِدَ لِلَّهِ} وَ{أَنَّهُ لَمَا قَامَ عَبْدُ اللَّهِ} وَغَيْرِهِ. أَوْ كَانَ لِدَلَالَةِ أَنَّ مَا دُونَهُ كَانَ مُسْتَوْلَىً عَلَيْهِ بِالِاسْتِيلَاءِ عَلَيْهِ كَمَا يُقَال: فُلَانٌ أَمِيرُ هَذِهِ الْبَلْدَةِ وَإِنْ كَانَ أَمِيرًا لِأَهْلِهَا وَلِرَسَاتِيقِهَا وَقُرَاهَا قَالَ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى: {وَهُوَ رَبُّ الْعَرْشِ الْعَظِيمِ} وَإِنْ كَانَ رَبًّا لِكُلِّ شَيْءٍ وَهَذَا كُلُّهُ ظَاهِرٌ. وَتَزْيِيفُ الْأَشْعَرِيَّةِ هَذَا التَّأْوِيلَ لِمَكَانِ أَنَّ الِاسْتِيلَاءَ يَكُونُ بَعْدَ الضَّعْفِ لَا يُتَصَوَّرُ فِي اللَّهِ تَعَالَى وَنِسْبَتُهُمْ هَذَا التَّأْوِيلَ إِلَى الْمُعْتَزِلَةِ لَيْسَ بِشَيْءٍ لِأَنَّ أَصْحَابَنَا أَوَّلُوا هَذَا التَّأْوِيلَ وَلَمْ تَخْتَصَّ بِهِ الْمُعْتَزِلَةُ وَكَوْنُ الِاسْتِيلَاءِ إِنْ كَانَ فِي الشَّاهِدِ عَقِيبَ الضَّعْفِ وَلَكِنْ لَمْ يَكُنْ هَذَا عِبَارَةً عَنِ اسْتِيلَاءٍ عَنْ ضَعْفٍ فِي اللُّغَةِ بَلْ ذَلِكَ ثَبَتَ عَلَىٰ وِفَاقِ الْعَادَةِ. كَمَا يُقَال: عَلِمَ فُلَانٌ وَكَانَ ذَلِكَ فِي الْمَخْلُوقِينَ بَعْدَ الْجَهْلِ. وَيُقَال: قَدَرَ وَكَانَ ذَلِكَ بَعْدَ الْعَجْزِ وَهَذَا الْإِطْلَاقُ جَائِزٌ فِي اللَّهِ تَعَالَى عَلَىٰ إِرَادَةِ تَحَقُّقِ الْعِلْمِ وَالْقُدْرَةِ بِدُونِ سَابِقَةِ الْجَهْلِ وَالْعَجْزِ فَكَذَا هَذَا عَلَىٰ أَنَّ اللَّفْظَ الْمَوْضُوعَ لِمَعْنَيَيْنِ يَسْتَحِيلُ أَحَدُهُمَا عَلَى اللَّهِ تَعَالَى وَلَا يَسْتَحِيلُ الْآخَرُ يُفْهَمُ مِنْهُ إِذَا أُضِيفَ إِلَى اللَّهِ تَعَالَى مَا لَا يَسْتَحِيلُ عَلَيْهِ دُونَ مَا يَسْتَحِيلُ عَلَيْهِ كَلَفْظَةِ الْعَجَبِ فَإِنَّ الْعَجَبَ فِي اللُّغَةِ عِبَارَةٌ عَنِ اسْتِحْسَانِ الشَّيْءِ مَعَ الْجَهْلِ بِسَبَبِهِ فَإِذَا أُضِيفَ إِلَى اللَّهِ تَعَالَى فِي مِثْلِ قَوْلِهِ: {بَلْ عَجِبْتَ وَيَسْخَرُونَ} فِي قِرَاءَةِ مَنْ قَرَأَ بِضَمِّ التَّاءِ يُفْهَمُ مِنْهُ الِاسْتِحْسَانُ فَحَسْبُ فَفِي اللَّفْظِ الَّذِي مَا وُضِعَ لِلضَّعْفِ بَلْ وُضِعَ لِنَفَاذِ السَّلْطَنَةِ وَالتَّصَرُّفِ وَتَثْبُتُ فِيهِ سَابِقَةُ الضَّعْفِ لَا بِدَلَالَةِ اللَّفْظِ بَلْ بِوِفَاقِ الْعَادَةِ لِأَنْ لَا يُفْهَمَ مِنْهُ مَا يَسْتَحِيلُ عَلَى اللَّهِ أَوْلَى وَاللَّهُ الْمُوَفِّقُ. وَلَوْ أُرِيدَ بِالْعَرْشِ الْمُلْكُ لَكَانَ الِاسْتِوَاءُ عِبَارَةً عَنِ التَّمَامِ أَيْ تَمَامِ الْمَمْلُوكِ كَمَا رُوِيَ عَنِ ابْنِ عَبَّاسٍ رَضِيَ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى عَنْهُمَا أَنَّهُ تَعَالَى خَلَقَ الْعَالَمَ فِي سِتَّةِ أَيَّامٍ ثُمَّ خَلَقَ الْبَشَرَ فِي الْيَوْمِ السَّابِعِ فَإِنَّهُمُ التَّمَامُ إِذْ خَلَقَ لَهُمْ كُلَّ شَيْءٍ وَقَدْ بَالَغَ أَئِمَّةُ أَهْلِ السُّنَّةِ فِي بَيَانِ مَا تَحْتَمِلُ هَذِهِ الْآيَاتُ مِنَ التَّأْوِيلَاتِ الَّتِي لَا تُنَافِي التَّوْحِيدَ وَالْقِدَمَ. أَعْرَضْنَا عَنْ ذِكْرِهَا مَخَافَةَ التَّطْوِيلِ إِذْ فِي هَذَا الْقَدْرِ كِفَايَةٌ لِمَنْ نَصَحَ نَفْسَهُ. وَبِاللَّهِ الرَّشَادُ. وَقَالُوا فِي قَوْلِهِ تَعَالَى: {وَهُوَ الَّذِي فِي السَّمَاءِ اللَّهُ وَفِي الْأَرْضِ إِلَهٌ} أَرَادَ بِهِ ثُبُوتَ الْإِلَهِيَّةِ فِي السَّمَاءِ لَا ثُبُوتَ ذَاتِهِ كَمَا تَقُول: فُلَانٌ أَمِيرٌ بِبُخَارَىٰ وَسَمَرْقَنْدَ وَيُرَادُ
You and those with you have settled (استويت) — and this type divides into a division mentioned meaning elevation in terms of place, and mentioned meaning elevation in terms of rank. Thus, it is possible that what is meant by it is 'He gained mastery (استولى) over the Throne which is the greatest of creations, and its specific mention was an honor for it, since the attribution of particular things to Allah is to magnify that, as He said: 'the she-camel of Allah' and 'that the mosques are for Allah' and 'that when the servant of Allah stood' and other than that. Or it was to indicate that what is below it was mastered by mastery over it, as it is said: 'So-and-so is the governor of this town' even though he is governor over its people and its districts and its villages. Allah said: 'And He is the Lord of the Mighty Throne' even though He is Lord of everything. And all of this is apparent. And the Ash'aris' falsification of this interpretation because of the fact that mastery (استيلاء) occurs after weakness, which is not conceivable in Allah, and their attribution of this interpretation to the Mu'tazila is nothing, because our associates (أصحابنا) interpreted this interpretation, and the Mu'tazila were not unique to it. And that mastery, even if in the witnessed world it is subsequent to weakness, but this was not an expression for mastery from weakness in the language; rather that is established in accordance with custom. Just as it is said: 'So-and-so knew' and that was among created beings after ignorance. And it is said: 'He had power' and that was after incapacity. And this usage is permissible with respect to Allah intending the realization of knowledge and power without preceding ignorance and incapacity. So likewise this. Moreover, the word set for two meanings, one of which is impossible for Allah and the other not impossible, is understood from it, when attributed to Allah, what is not impossible for Him, not what is impossible for Him, like the word 'wonder' (العجب); for wonder in the language is an expression for approval of something along with ignorance of its cause. So when it is attributed to Allah in such as His saying: 'But you wondered and they mock' in the reading of whoever read with a damma on the ta, it is understood as approval only. So in the word that was not set for weakness but set for the penetration of sovereignty and disposal, and in which precedence of weakness is established not by the indication of the word but by accordance with custom, it is more fitting that not be understood from it what is impossible for Allah. And Allah is the Grantor of success. And if by 'Throne' sovereignty (الملك) is meant, then 'istiwa' (استواء) is an expression for completion, i.e., completion of the possessed, as narrated from Ibn Abbas, may Allah be pleased with them both: 'He created the world in six days, then created humans on the seventh day, for they are the completion, since He created everything for them.' And the imams of Ahl al-Sunna have elaborated in explaining what these verses admit of interpretations that do not contradict tawhid and preeternity. We have refrained from mentioning them out of fear of prolixity, since this amount is sufficient for one who advises himself. And with Allah is guidance. And they said concerning His saying: 'And He is the one who in heaven is Allah and on earth is the God' — He meant by it the establishment of divinity (الألوهية) in heaven, not the establishment of His essence, as you say: 'So-and-so is an emir in Bukhara and Samarkand' and is meant
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage is an extended defense of the interpretation of the Quranic verse 'الرحمن على العرش استوى' as meaning Allah's dominion and mastery (استيلاء) over the Throne, rather than a literal physical settling. The author begins by classifying the concept of 'uluww (elevation) into two types: locative elevation and rank elevation. He then applies this to the verse, suggesting that 'استوى' here means 'استولى' (gained mastery), and that the specific mention of the Throne is for honor, just as Allah specifically mentions 'the she-camel of Allah' or 'the servant of Allah' to magnify them. He also draws an analogy: just as saying 'so-and-so is the governor of this town' does not negate that he governs the surrounding areas, so Allah's being 'Lord of the Mighty Throne' does not contradict His being Lord of everything. The author then refutes the Ash'ari objection that 'استيلاء' implies prior weakness, which is impossible for Allah. He argues that this interpretation is not exclusive to the Mu'tazila, as the author's own school (Maturidis) also holds it. He explains that although in human experience mastery often follows weakness, the word itself in the Arabic language does not necessarily imply that; rather, the temporal sequence is a customary association, not a linguistic necessity. He gives parallel examples: words like 'عَلِمَ' (knew) and 'قَدَرَ' (was able) are used for Allah even though they imply prior ignorance or incapacity when applied to creatures; but when applied to Allah, they mean the perfection of knowledge and power without any prior deficiency. Similarly, the word 'استيلاء' should be understood as perfect sovereignty without any prior weakness. He then uses the example of the word 'عجب' (wonder), which in human language implies approval mixed with ignorance, but when attributed to Allah in the Quran (in the reading 'بل عجبت'), it means only approval, without ignorance. Thus, for a word that can bear two meanings—one impossible for Allah and one possible—the possible meaning is adopted when the word is attributed to Allah. The author then presents an alternative interpretation: if 'العرش' means 'الملك' (sovereignty), then 'الاستواء' means 'التمام' (completion), as reported from Ibn Abbas: after creating the world in six days, Allah created humans on the seventh day, completing the creation for their benefit. The author concludes by noting that Ahl al-Sunna imams have extensively discussed permissible interpretations that do not compromise tawhid and divine preeternity, but he refrains from further detail. Finally, he turns to the verse 'وهو الذي في السماء إله وفي الأرض إله', explaining that it means the establishment of divinity and lordship in heaven and earth, not the physical location of Allah's essence. He gives the analogy of saying 'so-and-so is an emir in Bukhara and Samarkand', meaning he exercises authority there, not that his body is present in both places simultaneously.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if a word commonly occurs after a certain condition in human language, that condition is part of its core meaning and must apply to Allah.
Correction: Nasafi shows that customary associations are not part of the word's definition; they are circumstantial. Therefore, such words can be used for Allah without implying those conditions.

Checkpoint
In refuting the Ash'ari objection, Imam Nasafi uses the example of which word to illustrate the principle that a word's customary association does not affect its meaning when attributed to Allah?
A) Knowledge ('ilm)
B) Strength (quwwa)
C) Servant ('abd)
D) Sitting (jalsa)

Key Takeaway
The meaning of a word when applied to Allah is determined by its lexical sense, not by customary human circumstances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that Allah 'knows' or 'has power,' do you ever subconsciously imagine that His knowledge came after a time of not knowing, or His power after weakness? How can you actively train your mind to strip away these human limitations when thinking of Allah?
?
The linguistic principle in this lesson teaches that the meaning of a word for Allah is determined by its lexical sense, not by how it occurs in human experience. In what areas of your life—beliefs, actions, or worship—are you letting human customs or experiences define what you expect from Allah, rather than letting His revelation define itself?
?
Imagine you hear someone say that a certain attribute cannot apply to Allah because it always involves a deficiency in humans. Based on this passage, what would you say to them? And more importantly, are there any attributes of Allah that you personally struggle to affirm because your own experience taints the meaning?

Divine Transcendence
intermediate
5 min
Alternative Interpretation: Istiwa as Completion and the Verse on Divinity
Nasafi presents an alternative interpretation and explains a verse on divine lordship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the alternative interpretation of 'istiwa' as completion (tamam) when 'arsh means sovereignty, and the correct understanding of the verse about Allah being the God in heaven and earth.

Core Idea
Istiwa can also mean completion of sovereignty, and the verse 'Allah in heaven and earth' means His divinity is established everywhere, not His physical presence.

Imam Nasafi presents another possible interpretation: if by 'Throne' is meant sovereignty (mulk), then 'istiwa' can mean completion (tamam), as reported from Ibn Abbas. The creation of humans on the seventh day completed the creation for their benefit. He then briefly mentions that Ahl al-Sunna imams have detailed many such interpretations. Finally, he addresses the verse 'He is the one who in heaven is Allah and on earth is the God,' clarifying that it refers to the establishment of divinity (uluhiyya), not the physical location of Allah's essence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the 'istiwa as completion' interpretation
Nasafi cites Ibn Abbas: Allah created the world in six days, then created humans on the seventh day as the completion. So if 'arsh means sovereignty, then 'istiwa' means the completion of that sovereignty.
This is another legitimate interpretation that avoids anthropomorphism.
2
Recognize the brevity of the author
Nasafi notes that he could elaborate more but refrains for brevity, stating that this much is sufficient for one who advises his soul.
This shows his pedagogical intent.
3
Interpret the verse with 'ilaha'
He explains the verse 'Wa huwa alladhi fi al-sama'i ilah wa fi al-ardi ilah' means that Allah's divinity is established in heaven and earth, not that His essence is physically present there. He gives the analogy of a ruler being emir in two cities.
This clarifies a common misunderstanding about divine omnipresence.

Source Text

اسْتَوَيْتَ أَنتَ وَمَن مَّعَكَ وَهَذَا النَّوْعُ يَنْقَسِمُ إِلَى قَسِيمٍ يُذْكَرُ وَيُرَادُ بِهِ الْعُلُوُّ مِنْ حَيْثُ الْمَكَانِ وَيُذْكَرُ وَيُرَادُ بِهِ الْعُلُوُّ مِنْ حَيْثُ الرُّتْبَةِ. فَعَلَى هَذَا فَيَحْتَمِلُ أَنْ يَكُونَ الْمُرَادُ مِنْهُ اسْتَوْلَى عَلَى الْعَرْشِ الَّذِي هُوَ أَعْظَمُ الْمَخْلُوقَاتِ وَتَخْصِيصُهُ بِالذِّكْرِ كَانَ تَشْرِيفًا لَهُ إِذْ إِضَافَةُ جُزْئِيَّةِ الْأَشْيَاءِ إِلَى اللَّهِ تَعَالَى لِيَتَعَظَّمَ ذَلِكَ كَمَا قَالَ: {نَاقَةَ اللَّهِ} وَ{أَنَّ الْمَسَاجِدَ لِلَّهِ} وَ{أَنَّهُ لَمَا قَامَ عَبْدُ اللَّهِ} وَغَيْرِهِ. أَوْ كَانَ لِدَلَالَةِ أَنَّ مَا دُونَهُ كَانَ مُسْتَوْلَىً عَلَيْهِ بِالِاسْتِيلَاءِ عَلَيْهِ كَمَا يُقَال: فُلَانٌ أَمِيرُ هَذِهِ الْبَلْدَةِ وَإِنْ كَانَ أَمِيرًا لِأَهْلِهَا وَلِرَسَاتِيقِهَا وَقُرَاهَا قَالَ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى: {وَهُوَ رَبُّ الْعَرْشِ الْعَظِيمِ} وَإِنْ كَانَ رَبًّا لِكُلِّ شَيْءٍ وَهَذَا كُلُّهُ ظَاهِرٌ. وَتَزْيِيفُ الْأَشْعَرِيَّةِ هَذَا التَّأْوِيلَ لِمَكَانِ أَنَّ الِاسْتِيلَاءَ يَكُونُ بَعْدَ الضَّعْفِ لَا يُتَصَوَّرُ فِي اللَّهِ تَعَالَى وَنِسْبَتُهُمْ هَذَا التَّأْوِيلَ إِلَى الْمُعْتَزِلَةِ لَيْسَ بِشَيْءٍ لِأَنَّ أَصْحَابَنَا أَوَّلُوا هَذَا التَّأْوِيلَ وَلَمْ تَخْتَصَّ بِهِ الْمُعْتَزِلَةُ وَكَوْنُ الِاسْتِيلَاءِ إِنْ كَانَ فِي الشَّاهِدِ عَقِيبَ الضَّعْفِ وَلَكِنْ لَمْ يَكُنْ هَذَا عِبَارَةً عَنِ اسْتِيلَاءٍ عَنْ ضَعْفٍ فِي اللُّغَةِ بَلْ ذَلِكَ ثَبَتَ عَلَىٰ وِفَاقِ الْعَادَةِ. كَمَا يُقَال: عَلِمَ فُلَانٌ وَكَانَ ذَلِكَ فِي الْمَخْلُوقِينَ بَعْدَ الْجَهْلِ. وَيُقَال: قَدَرَ وَكَانَ ذَلِكَ بَعْدَ الْعَجْزِ وَهَذَا الْإِطْلَاقُ جَائِزٌ فِي اللَّهِ تَعَالَى عَلَىٰ إِرَادَةِ تَحَقُّقِ الْعِلْمِ وَالْقُدْرَةِ بِدُونِ سَابِقَةِ الْجَهْلِ وَالْعَجْزِ فَكَذَا هَذَا عَلَىٰ أَنَّ اللَّفْظَ الْمَوْضُوعَ لِمَعْنَيَيْنِ يَسْتَحِيلُ أَحَدُهُمَا عَلَى اللَّهِ تَعَالَى وَلَا يَسْتَحِيلُ الْآخَرُ يُفْهَمُ مِنْهُ إِذَا أُضِيفَ إِلَى اللَّهِ تَعَالَى مَا لَا يَسْتَحِيلُ عَلَيْهِ دُونَ مَا يَسْتَحِيلُ عَلَيْهِ كَلَفْظَةِ الْعَجَبِ فَإِنَّ الْعَجَبَ فِي اللُّغَةِ عِبَارَةٌ عَنِ اسْتِحْسَانِ الشَّيْءِ مَعَ الْجَهْلِ بِسَبَبِهِ فَإِذَا أُضِيفَ إِلَى اللَّهِ تَعَالَى فِي مِثْلِ قَوْلِهِ: {بَلْ عَجِبْتَ وَيَسْخَرُونَ} فِي قِرَاءَةِ مَنْ قَرَأَ بِضَمِّ التَّاءِ يُفْهَمُ مِنْهُ الِاسْتِحْسَانُ فَحَسْبُ فَفِي اللَّفْظِ الَّذِي مَا وُضِعَ لِلضَّعْفِ بَلْ وُضِعَ لِنَفَاذِ السَّلْطَنَةِ وَالتَّصَرُّفِ وَتَثْبُتُ فِيهِ سَابِقَةُ الضَّعْفِ لَا بِدَلَالَةِ اللَّفْظِ بَلْ بِوِفَاقِ الْعَادَةِ لِأَنْ لَا يُفْهَمَ مِنْهُ مَا يَسْتَحِيلُ عَلَى اللَّهِ أَوْلَى وَاللَّهُ الْمُوَفِّقُ. وَلَوْ أُرِيدَ بِالْعَرْشِ الْمُلْكُ لَكَانَ الِاسْتِوَاءُ عِبَارَةً عَنِ التَّمَامِ أَيْ تَمَامِ الْمَمْلُوكِ كَمَا رُوِيَ عَنِ ابْنِ عَبَّاسٍ رَضِيَ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى عَنْهُمَا أَنَّهُ تَعَالَى خَلَقَ الْعَالَمَ فِي سِتَّةِ أَيَّامٍ ثُمَّ خَلَقَ الْبَشَرَ فِي الْيَوْمِ السَّابِعِ فَإِنَّهُمُ التَّمَامُ إِذْ خَلَقَ لَهُمْ كُلَّ شَيْءٍ وَقَدْ بَالَغَ أَئِمَّةُ أَهْلِ السُّنَّةِ فِي بَيَانِ مَا تَحْتَمِلُ هَذِهِ الْآيَاتُ مِنَ التَّأْوِيلَاتِ الَّتِي لَا تُنَافِي التَّوْحِيدَ وَالْقِدَمَ. أَعْرَضْنَا عَنْ ذِكْرِهَا مَخَافَةَ التَّطْوِيلِ إِذْ فِي هَذَا الْقَدْرِ كِفَايَةٌ لِمَنْ نَصَحَ نَفْسَهُ. وَبِاللَّهِ الرَّشَادُ. وَقَالُوا فِي قَوْلِهِ تَعَالَى: {وَهُوَ الَّذِي فِي السَّمَاءِ اللَّهُ وَفِي الْأَرْضِ إِلَهٌ} أَرَادَ بِهِ ثُبُوتَ الْإِلَهِيَّةِ فِي السَّمَاءِ لَا ثُبُوتَ ذَاتِهِ كَمَا تَقُول: فُلَانٌ أَمِيرٌ بِبُخَارَىٰ وَسَمَرْقَنْدَ وَيُرَادُ
You and those with you have settled (استويت) — and this type divides into a division mentioned meaning elevation in terms of place, and mentioned meaning elevation in terms of rank. Thus, it is possible that what is meant by it is 'He gained mastery (استولى) over the Throne which is the greatest of creations, and its specific mention was an honor for it, since the attribution of particular things to Allah is to magnify that, as He said: 'the she-camel of Allah' and 'that the mosques are for Allah' and 'that when the servant of Allah stood' and other than that. Or it was to indicate that what is below it was mastered by mastery over it, as it is said: 'So-and-so is the governor of this town' even though he is governor over its people and its districts and its villages. Allah said: 'And He is the Lord of the Mighty Throne' even though He is Lord of everything. And all of this is apparent. And the Ash'aris' falsification of this interpretation because of the fact that mastery (استيلاء) occurs after weakness, which is not conceivable in Allah, and their attribution of this interpretation to the Mu'tazila is nothing, because our associates (أصحابنا) interpreted this interpretation, and the Mu'tazila were not unique to it. And that mastery, even if in the witnessed world it is subsequent to weakness, but this was not an expression for mastery from weakness in the language; rather that is established in accordance with custom. Just as it is said: 'So-and-so knew' and that was among created beings after ignorance. And it is said: 'He had power' and that was after incapacity. And this usage is permissible with respect to Allah intending the realization of knowledge and power without preceding ignorance and incapacity. So likewise this. Moreover, the word set for two meanings, one of which is impossible for Allah and the other not impossible, is understood from it, when attributed to Allah, what is not impossible for Him, not what is impossible for Him, like the word 'wonder' (العجب); for wonder in the language is an expression for approval of something along with ignorance of its cause. So when it is attributed to Allah in such as His saying: 'But you wondered and they mock' in the reading of whoever read with a damma on the ta, it is understood as approval only. So in the word that was not set for weakness but set for the penetration of sovereignty and disposal, and in which precedence of weakness is established not by the indication of the word but by accordance with custom, it is more fitting that not be understood from it what is impossible for Allah. And Allah is the Grantor of success. And if by 'Throne' sovereignty (الملك) is meant, then 'istiwa' (استواء) is an expression for completion, i.e., completion of the possessed, as narrated from Ibn Abbas, may Allah be pleased with them both: 'He created the world in six days, then created humans on the seventh day, for they are the completion, since He created everything for them.' And the imams of Ahl al-Sunna have elaborated in explaining what these verses admit of interpretations that do not contradict tawhid and preeternity. We have refrained from mentioning them out of fear of prolixity, since this amount is sufficient for one who advises himself. And with Allah is guidance. And they said concerning His saying: 'And He is the one who in heaven is Allah and on earth is the God' — He meant by it the establishment of divinity (الألوهية) in heaven, not the establishment of His essence, as you say: 'So-and-so is an emir in Bukhara and Samarkand' and is meant
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage is an extended defense of the interpretation of the Quranic verse 'الرحمن على العرش استوى' as meaning Allah's dominion and mastery (استيلاء) over the Throne, rather than a literal physical settling. The author begins by classifying the concept of 'uluww (elevation) into two types: locative elevation and rank elevation. He then applies this to the verse, suggesting that 'استوى' here means 'استولى' (gained mastery), and that the specific mention of the Throne is for honor, just as Allah specifically mentions 'the she-camel of Allah' or 'the servant of Allah' to magnify them. He also draws an analogy: just as saying 'so-and-so is the governor of this town' does not negate that he governs the surrounding areas, so Allah's being 'Lord of the Mighty Throne' does not contradict His being Lord of everything. The author then refutes the Ash'ari objection that 'استيلاء' implies prior weakness, which is impossible for Allah. He argues that this interpretation is not exclusive to the Mu'tazila, as the author's own school (Maturidis) also holds it. He explains that although in human experience mastery often follows weakness, the word itself in the Arabic language does not necessarily imply that; rather, the temporal sequence is a customary association, not a linguistic necessity. He gives parallel examples: words like 'عَلِمَ' (knew) and 'قَدَرَ' (was able) are used for Allah even though they imply prior ignorance or incapacity when applied to creatures; but when applied to Allah, they mean the perfection of knowledge and power without any prior deficiency. Similarly, the word 'استيلاء' should be understood as perfect sovereignty without any prior weakness. He then uses the example of the word 'عجب' (wonder), which in human language implies approval mixed with ignorance, but when attributed to Allah in the Quran (in the reading 'بل عجبت'), it means only approval, without ignorance. Thus, for a word that can bear two meanings—one impossible for Allah and one possible—the possible meaning is adopted when the word is attributed to Allah. The author then presents an alternative interpretation: if 'العرش' means 'الملك' (sovereignty), then 'الاستواء' means 'التمام' (completion), as reported from Ibn Abbas: after creating the world in six days, Allah created humans on the seventh day, completing the creation for their benefit. The author concludes by noting that Ahl al-Sunna imams have extensively discussed permissible interpretations that do not compromise tawhid and divine preeternity, but he refrains from further detail. Finally, he turns to the verse 'وهو الذي في السماء إله وفي الأرض إله', explaining that it means the establishment of divinity and lordship in heaven and earth, not the physical location of Allah's essence. He gives the analogy of saying 'so-and-so is an emir in Bukhara and Samarkand', meaning he exercises authority there, not that his body is present in both places simultaneously.

Key Terms
العرش
الملك
التمام
الألوهية
السما
الأرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'He is Allah in heaven and Allah on earth' means that Allah is physically located in heaven and on earth simultaneously.
Correction: Nasafi clarifies that it means His divinity and lordship are recognized in both realms, similar to how a ruler is called 'emir' in different cities.

Checkpoint
According to the interpretation reported from Ibn Abbas, what does 'istiwa' mean if 'arsh' is understood as 'mulk' (sovereignty)?
A) Physical sitting
B) Grasping and taking control
C) Completion
D) Ascension

Key Takeaway
The verse 'Allah in heaven and earth' refers to the establishment of His divinity, not His physical presence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the verse 'He is Allah in heaven and on earth,' do you ever catch yourself thinking of Allah as confined to a location? How does that conflict with the tawhid you profess?
?
The imams of Ahl al-Sunna labored to protect tawhid through careful interpretation. Are you willing to submit your own understanding to their guidance, or do you trust your intuition over centuries of scholarly consensus?
?
The analogy of an emir's authority in two cities shows that Allah's divinity is established everywhere. Is your daily life a testimony that Allah has authority over every domain, or do you live as if some areas are outside His sovereignty?

Nasafi's Ta'wil of Anthropomorphic Verses
Nasafi interprets verses that might imply Allah's location or movement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Nasafi interprets Qur'anic verses that might imply Allah has a physical location or direction, using ta'wil to uphold divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Qur'anic verses about Allah's 'location' refer to His sovereignty, knowledge, or power, not His essence.

When reading the Qur'an, we encounter verses that seem to describe Allah as being in a specific place or moving. How do we understand these without falling into anthropomorphism? In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi provides a series of exegetical interpretations (ta'wil) that show each such verse actually refers to Allah's attributes—His authority, knowledge, or power—not His physical presence. Let's follow his reasoning verse by verse.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the full English translation. Get a sense of the flow of Nasafi's arguments.
Notice that Nasafi uses a consistent pattern for each verse.
2
Identify the interpretive principle
Observe how Nasafi distinguishes between Allah's essence (dhat) and His attributes or actions. For each verse, he replaces spatial language with a reference to knowledge, power, sovereignty, or rank.
3
Work through each verse example
Go verse by verse: note the apparent meaning that could imply location, then Nasafi's ta'wil. See how each interpretation preserves divine transcendence.
There are seven verses covered. Take them one at a time.
4
Reflect on the theological necessity
Ask yourself why this method is important. How does it protect tawhid and prevent likening Allah to creation?
This connects to the broader Islamic principle of tanzih (transcendence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
به أن أمارته وسلطنته فيهما لا ذاته، وكذا هذا في قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَهُوَ اللَّهُ فِي السَّمَوَاتِ وَفِي الأرْضِ ﴾ أي ألوهيته فيهما لا ذاته. وكذا في قوله تعالى: ﴿ أَمِنْتُم مِّن فِي السَّمَاء ﴾ أي في السماء ألوهيته إلا أنها أضمرت لدلالة ما سبق من الآيات. وقوله تعالى ﴿ مَا يَكُونُ مِن نَجْوَى ثَلَاثَةٍ إِلَّا هُوَ رَابِعُهُمْ ﴾ أي: يعلم ذلك ولا يخفى عليه. وقوله تعالى ﴿ وَنَحْنُ أَقْرَبُ إِلَيْهِ مِنْ حَبْلِ الْوَرِيدِ ﴾ أي: بالسلطان والقدرة. وقوله: فوق كل شيء أي بالقهر على ما قال: ﴿ وَهُوَ الْقَاهِرُ فَوْقَ عِبَادِهِ ﴾. وقالوا في تعلقهم بقوله: ﴿ إِلَيْهِ يَصْعَدُ الْكَلِمُ الطَّيِّبُ ﴾ إن الله تعالى جعل ديوان أعمال العباد في السماء والحفظة من الملائكة فيها، فيكون ما رفع هناك كأنه رفع إليه لأنه أمر بذلك. كما قال إبراهيم عليه السلام: ﴿ إِنِّي ذَاهِبٌ إِلَى رَبِّي ﴾ أي: إلى الموضع الذي أمرني أن أذهب إليه، وكما قال عز وجل: ﴿ وَمَن يَخْرُجُ مِن بَيْتِهِ ربي مُهَاجِرًا إِلَى اللَّهِ وَرَسُولِهِ ﴾. والله أعلم. وقالوا في تعلقهم بقوله تعالى: ﴿ إِنَّ الَّذِينَ عِندَ رَبِّكَ لَا يَسْتَكْبِرُونَ ﴾ يعني الملائكة، أن المراد منه المنزلة لا المكان. كما قال في موسى عليه السلام: ﴿ وَكَانَ عِندَ اللَّهِ وَجِيهًا ﴾. وقال: ﴿ إِنَّ الدِّينَ عِندَ اللَّهِ الْإِسْلَامُ ﴾ أي هو الحق الذي له منزلة عند الله تعالى في الأديان. والله الموفق.
By it: that His sovereignty and authority are in them, not His essence. Likewise this in His saying the Exalted: 'And He is Allah in the heavens and the earth' meaning His divinity is in them, not His essence. Likewise in His saying the Exalted: 'Do you feel secure from Him who is in the heaven' meaning in the heaven is His divinity, but it is implied due to the indication of the previous verses. And His saying the Exalted: 'There is not a secret conversation of three except that He is the fourth of them' meaning He knows that and it is not hidden from Him. And His saying the Exalted: 'And We are closer to him than the jugular vein' meaning by authority and power. And His saying: 'above everything' meaning by domination, as He said: 'And He is the Subjugator over His servants.' And they said in their adherence to His saying: 'To Him ascends the good word' that Allah the Exalted placed the record of the deeds of the servants in the heaven and the guardian angels are in it, so what is raised there is as if it is raised to Him because He commanded that. As Ibrahim, peace be upon him, said: 'Indeed I am going to my Lord' meaning to the place which He commanded me to go to, and as He said, Mighty and Majestic: 'And whoever leaves his house as an emigrant to Allah and His Messenger.' And Allah knows best. And they said in their adherence to His saying the Exalted: 'Indeed those who are with your Lord are not arrogant' meaning the angels, that what is meant by it is rank, not place. As He said about Moses, peace be upon him: 'And he was honored with Allah.' And He said: 'Indeed the religion with Allah is Islam' meaning it is the truth which has a rank with Allah the Exalted among the religions. And Allah is the giver of success.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage is a series of exegetical interpretations (ta'wil) of Qur'anic verses that might be misunderstood to imply that Allah has a physical location or direction. Nasafi refutes such interpretations by explaining each verse metaphorically. He begins with verse 6:3 'And He is Allah in the heavens and the earth'—he explains that it means Allah's divinity (uluhiyya) and sovereignty are manifest in both realms, not His essence. Similarly for verse 67:16 'Do you feel secure from Him who is in the heaven', the phrase 'in the heaven' refers to His divinity, not His person. Next, verse 58:7 about being the fourth of three is interpreted as knowledge, not physical presence. Verse 50:16 about being closer than the jugular vein is interpreted as His power and authority (sultan wa qudra). The phrase 'above everything' in verse 6:18 is interpreted as domination (qahr). Regarding verse 35:10 'To Him ascends the good word', he says that Allah placed the register of deeds in heaven and the guardian angels there, so raising deeds is like raising to Him by command, as in the story of Abraham's emigration (37:99) and general emigration (4:100). For verse 7:206 'those who are with your Lord' referring to angels, it means rank (manzila), not place, similar to Moses being 'honored with Allah' (33:69) and verse 3:19 'the religion with Allah is Islam' meaning it is the true religion that has a high status with Allah. Each case demonstrates that when attributes are applied to Allah, they must be understood in a way befitting His majesty, avoiding any implication of corporeality.

ألوهيته
بالسلطان والقدرة
بالقهر
المنزلة لا المكان
تأويل

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that verses like 'He is with you' imply Allah's physical presence everywhere.
Correction: Nasafi clarifies that such verses refer to His knowledge, power, or authority, not His essence.

Key Takeaway
To safeguard Allah's transcendence, any verse that appears to describe Him in spatial terms must be interpreted as referring to His attributes—such as knowledge, power, or authority—not His essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'He is with you' in verse 57:4, do you feel the weight of His knowledge of every secret thought? Will you live today as if He is watching, because He truly knows?
?
You have likely thought about Allah 'above' as a direction. Now that you understand it means domination, does your heart submit to His absolute authority over your life? What area of your life still resists His qahr?
?
In your hardest moments, you may have cried out feeling Allah is far. Now you know He is nearer than your own vein through His power. How will you carry this reality into your next difficulty and turn to Him with confidence?

The Hermeneutic Principle for Interpreting Divine Attributes
Nasafi lays down the hermeneutic principle for interpreting attributes of Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the general principle that words attributed to Allah must be interpreted in ways consistent with His transcendence, either by interpretation (ta'wil) or entrusting (tafwid).

Core Idea
Words reveal their meaning based on who or what they are attributed to.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi lays down a foundational principle for all theological discourse on divine attributes. Before engaging with specific verses, we need a clear method: what does it mean when we say 'Allah's hand' or 'Allah came'? Nasafi provides a rational and linguistic rule that governs how we understand such attributions, preserving both the revealed text and divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through.
Notice that Nasafi begins with 'wa ba'du' (and after this), a typical transition.
2
Identify the general principle
Nasafi states that any word attributed to a thing is understood according to what is possible (ja'iz) for that thing, and what is impossible (mustahil) is not understood.
This is a universal linguistic principle, not just for theology.
3
Examine the examples
The examples of 'Zayd came' and 'news came' show how the same verb 'came' yields different meanings because the subjects (Zayd is a body, news is an event) have different possibilities.
Physical movement is possible for Zayd but impossible for news; for news, 'came' means 'appeared'.
4
Apply to Allah
Apply the same principle to words attributed to Allah: we must not understand anything impossible for Him (e.g., physical movement), and we must either reinterpret the word in a way that is possible for Allah or entrust the meaning to Allah while affirming the text.
This leads to the two main approaches of the Salaf: ta'wil and tafwid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد فإن كل لفظ أضيف إلى شيء يفهم منه ما يجوز على ذلك الشيء ولا يستحيل، ولا يفهم منه ما يستحيل عليه. ألا ترى أن الرجل إذا قال: أتاني زيد، فهم منه الانتقال من مكان إلى مكان، لأن زيدا جسم ويجوز عليه الانتقال. وإذا قال: أتاني خبر فلان؛ لا يفهم منه الانتقال من مكان إلى مكان، لأن ذلك مما يستحيل على الخبر ففهم منه الظهور. وإذا ثبت هذا؛ فلا يجوز أن يفهم مما أضيف من الألفاظ إلى الله تعالى ما يستحيل عليه، ويجب صرفه إلى ما لا يستحيل عليه، أو تفويض المراد إليه، والإيمان بظاهر التنزيل مع صيانة العقيدة عما يوجب شيئا من أمارات الحدوث فيه. والله الموفق. وللمتكلمين في تأويل هذه الآيات كلام كثير وصنفوا في ذلك كتبا على حدة، إلا أنا اقتصرنا على هذا القدر لحصول المقصود، فمن أراد الزيادة فلينظر في تلك الكتب. والله الموفق.
And after this, every word that is added to something is understood from it what is permissible for that thing and not impossible, and what is impossible for it is not understood from it. Do you not see that when a man says: 'Zayd came to me', understood from it is movement from place to place, because Zayd is a body and movement is permissible for him. And when he says: 'The news of so-and-so came to me', movement from place to place is not understood from it, because that is impossible for news, so what is understood from it is its appearance. And when this is established, it is not permissible to understand from the words that are added to Allah the Exalted what is impossible for Him, and it is necessary to turn it to what is not impossible for Him, or to entrust the intended meaning to Him, and to believe in the apparent revelation while safeguarding the creed from anything that necessitates any of the signs of temporality in Him. And Allah is the giver of success. And the theologians have much speech in the interpretation of these verses, and they composed separate books on that, except that we limited ourselves to this amount for the attainment of the purpose, so whoever wants more, let him look into those books. And Allah is the giver of success.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage establishes a general hermeneutic principle for interpreting attributes ascribed to Allah. Nasafi begins by stating that any word attributed to a thing is understood in a way that is possible (ja'iz) for that thing, and impossible (mustahil) attributes are not understood. He gives two examples: when one says 'Zayd came to me', we understand physical movement because Zayd is a body (jism) and movement is possible for him. When one says 'the news of so-and-so came to me', we do not understand physical movement because that is impossible for news; instead we understand its appearance or arrival. Based on this principle, when words are added to Allah, we must not understand anything impossible for Him (like physical movement, location, change). Instead, we must either interpret them in a way that is possible for Allah (ta'wil) or entrust the meaning to Allah (tafwid), while affirming the literal wording of revelation but safeguarding the creed from any implication of createdness (hadath). Nasafi then notes that theologians have written extensively on ta'wil of these verses, and he has limited himself to this summary. This passage is a clear statement of the Maturidi approach to anthropomorphic verses: prioritize tanzih (transcendence) and use ta'wil or tafwid to avoid any impermissible attributions.

Key Terms
لفظ أضيف
يجوز
يستحيل
ظاهر التنزيل
أمارات الحدوث
تفويض المراد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'tafwid' means stopping at the literal meaning without understanding or affirming the text.
Correction: Nasafi clarifies that one must combine affirming the apparent revelation (zahir al-tanzil) with safeguarding the creed from any implication of temporality. Tafwid is not mere ignorance but a conscious entrusting of meaning to Allah while acknowledging that the literal sense cannot apply to Him.

Checkpoint
What does Nasafi say must be done when a word is added to Allah that might imply something impossible for Him?
A) Reject the word entirely.
B) Understand it literally without interpretation.
C) Turn it to what is not impossible or entrust the meaning to Allah.
D) Only use the principle of tafwid.

Key Takeaway
When interpreting divine attributes, we must avoid any meaning that implies imperfection or temporality in Allah, either by interpreting the word in a way befitting Him or by entrusting the true meaning to Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses like 'the hand of Allah is above their hands' (Qur'an 48:10), do you feel a tension between affirming the words and avoiding a physical image? How does this lesson's principle help you resolve that tension personally?
?
Which approach do you find more difficult: ta'wil (interpreting the attribute) or tafwid (entrusting the meaning to Allah)? What does your preference reveal about your struggle with transcendence?
?
If you were to catch yourself mentally picturing Allah moving or changing, how would you actively apply Nasafi's principle in that moment to correct your thought?

The Linguistic Principle for Interpreting Attributed Words
Nasafi presents a linguistic principle with two illustrative examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the meaning of a word shifts based on the nature of the thing it is attributed to, as illustrated by everyday examples.

Core Idea
Context determines possibility: what is permissible for a body is impossible for abstract news.

Nasafi begins with a simple linguistic observation: when we hear a word like 'came,' our understanding depends on what the subject is. If Zaid comes, we imagine movement; if news comes, we understand its appearance. This everyday reasoning forms the foundation for interpreting divine attributes, as he will show next.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the general rule
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Find the statement that encapsulates the principle: every word attributed to a thing conveys what is permissible and not impossible for that thing.
2
Study the two examples
Notice how the same verb 'came' is understood differently for Zaid (movement) and for news (appearance). Identify why this difference occurs based on the nature of the subject.
Zaid is a body; news is abstract.
3
Reflect on the significance for theology
Ask yourself: if this rule applies to all attributed words, how should we understand words attributed to God? This prepares you for the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد فإن كل لفظ أضيف إلى شيء يفهم منه ما يجوز على ذلك الشيء. ولا يستحيل، ولا يفهم منه ما يستحيل عليه، ألا ترى أن الرجل إذا قال: أتاني زيد، فهم منه الانتقال من مكان إلى مكان، لأن زيدا جسم ويجوز عليه الانتقال. وإذا قال: أتاني خبر فلان ؛ لا يفهم منه الانتقال من مكان إلى مكان، لأن ذلك مما يستحيل على الخبر ففهم منه الظهور. وإذا ثبت هذا؛ فلا يجوز أن يفهم مما أضيف من الألفاظ إلى الله تعالى ما يستحيل عليه، ويجب صرفه إلى ما لا يستحيل عليه، أو تفويض المراد إليه، والإيمان بظاهر التنزيل مع صيانة العقيدة عما يوجب شيئا من أمارات الحدوث فيه، والله الموفق. وللمتكلمين في تأويل هذه الآيات كلام كثير وصنفوا في ذلك كتبا على حدة، إلا أنا اقتصرنا على هذا القدر لحصول المقصود، فمن أراد الزيادة فلينظر في تلك الكتب. والله الموفق.
And after: every word that is attributed to something, what is permissible and not impossible for that thing is understood from it, and what is impossible for it is not understood. Do you not see that if a man says: 'Zaid came,' movement from place to place is understood from it, because Zaid is a body and movement is permissible for him. And if he says: 'The news of so-and-so came,' movement from place to place is not understood from it, because that is impossible for news, so its appearance is understood from it. And when this is established, it is not permissible to understand from words attributed to God Almighty what is impossible for Him, and it is obligatory to turn it to what is not impossible for Him, or to consign the intended meaning to Him, and to believe in the apparent meaning of revelation while preserving the creed from anything that implies any signs of temporality in Him. And God is the giver of success. And the theologians have much speech concerning the interpretation of these verses, and they authored separate books on that, except that we restricted ourselves to this amount because the purpose is achieved. Whoever desires more, let him look into those books. And God is the giver of success.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage establishes a foundational principle for interpreting terms attributed to God. Nasafi begins with a general rule: every word attributed to a thing conveys what is permissible and not impossible for that thing, and does not convey what is impossible for it. He illustrates with two examples. First, if someone says 'Zaid came,' movement from place to place is understood because Zaid is a body and movement is possible for him. Second, if someone says 'the news of so-and-so came,' movement is not understood because news is abstract and cannot move; rather, the meaning is that the news became known (appearance). Nasafi then applies the rule to God: since God is transcendent and has no imperfection, any attribute that would imply impossibility (such as change, body, temporality) must be understood in a way consistent with His majesty, or the exact meaning must be consigned to God (tafwid). The apparent wording of revelation is affirmed, but the creed is protected from any suggestion of createdness. Finally, Nasafi notes that theologians have written extensively on interpreting such verses, but he suffices with this concise principle, referring the reader to standard works for more detail.

Key Terms
أضيف
يجوز
يستحيل
جسم
خبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, when someone says 'the news of so-and-so came,' what is understood?
A) Movement from place to place
B) The news became known
C) The news physically arrived
D) Nothing can be understood

Key Takeaway
The meaning of an attributed word is determined by what is possible or impossible for the subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse like 'And your Lord comes' (Quran 89:22), do you instinctively imagine a physical approach? What does this lesson demand you correct in that instinct?
?
How often do you apply this linguistic principle when reflecting on the Quranic attributes of Allah? If not, what is preventing you from trusting that the scholars' methodology is the safeguard against anthropomorphism?
?
You know that Allah is not a body. Yet when you hear 'The Most Merciful rose over the Throne,' does your heart still fall into imagining a movement? What will you do — starting today — to train yourself to consciously apply this rule every time you recite or hear such verses?

Applying the Rule to Divine Attributes: Tafwid and Affirmation
Nasafi applies the linguistic rule to divine attributes and outlines the methodology of tafwid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the rule of linguistic attribution applies to God's attributes, leading to the twin principles of tafwid (consigning meaning to God) and affirming the apparent text while protecting divine transcendence.

Core Idea
When words are attributed to God, we must avoid impossible meanings and either interpret or consign the meaning to Him.

Having established the general linguistic rule, Nasafi now applies it to divine attributes. Since God is absolutely transcendent, any literal meaning that implies imperfection or temporality is impossible. The solution is twofold: we either understand the attribute in a way consistent with God's majesty, or we consign its exact meaning to God while still affirming the revealed wording.

Guided Path
1
Read the application of the rule to God
Read the Arabic and translation from 'And when this is established' onward. Identify how the general rule is applied to God.
2
Identify the two options presented
Nasafi says it is obligatory to either turn the word to what is not impossible or consign the intended meaning to God (tafwid). Understand each option.
3
Understand 'iman bi-zahir al-tanzil'
This phrase means 'faith in the apparent meaning of revelation.' Nasafi insists we affirm the revealed text while protecting the creed from signs of temporality.
4
Appreciate the reference to other works
Nasafi notes that theologians have discussed this in detail. This lesson provides the foundational principle; further study is available in specialized books.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد فإن كل لفظ أضيف إلى شيء يفهم منه ما يجوز على ذلك الشيء. ولا يستحيل، ولا يفهم منه ما يستحيل عليه، ألا ترى أن الرجل إذا قال: أتاني زيد، فهم منه الانتقال من مكان إلى مكان، لأن زيدا جسم ويجوز عليه الانتقال. وإذا قال: أتاني خبر فلان ؛ لا يفهم منه الانتقال من مكان إلى مكان، لأن ذلك مما يستحيل على الخبر ففهم منه الظهور. وإذا ثبت هذا؛ فلا يجوز أن يفهم مما أضيف من الألفاظ إلى الله تعالى ما يستحيل عليه، ويجب صرفه إلى ما لا يستحيل عليه، أو تفويض المراد إليه، والإيمان بظاهر التنزيل مع صيانة العقيدة عما يوجب شيئا من أمارات الحدوث فيه، والله الموفق. وللمتكلمين في تأويل هذه الآيات كلام كثير وصنفوا في ذلك كتبا على حدة، إلا أنا اقتصرنا على هذا القدر لحصول المقصود، فمن أراد الزيادة فلينظر في تلك الكتب. والله الموفق.
And after: every word that is attributed to something, what is permissible and not impossible for that thing is understood from it, and what is impossible for it is not understood. Do you not see that if a man says: 'Zaid came,' movement from place to place is understood from it, because Zaid is a body and movement is permissible for him. And if he says: 'The news of so-and-so came,' movement from place to place is not understood from it, because that is impossible for news, so its appearance is understood from it. And God is the giver of success. And the theologians have much speech concerning the interpretation of these verses, and they authored separate books on that, except that we restricted ourselves to this amount because the purpose is achieved. Whoever desires more, let him look into those books. And God is the giver of success.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage establishes a foundational principle for interpreting terms attributed to God. Nasafi begins with a general rule: every word attributed to a thing conveys what is permissible and not impossible for that thing, and does not convey what is impossible for it. He illustrates with two examples. First, if someone says 'Zaid came,' movement from place to place is understood because Zaid is a body and movement is possible for him. Second, if someone says 'the news of so-and-so came,' movement is not understood because news is abstract and cannot move; rather, the meaning is that the news became known (appearance). The apparent wording of revelation is affirmed, but the creed is protected from any suggestion of createdness. Finally, Nasafi notes that theologians have written extensively on interpreting such verses, but he suffices with this concise principle, referring the reader to standard works for more detail.

Key Terms

يستحيل
تفويض
ظاهر التنزيل
أمارات الحدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think tafwid means we can ignore or reject the literal text entirely.
Correction: Nasafi insists on 'iman bi-zahir al-tanzil' — faith in the apparent text — while protecting divine transcendence. Tafwid is about consigning the exact meaning, not rejecting the words.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what must be done when a word attributed to God implies something impossible for Him?
A) Reject the word as corrupted
B) Accept the impossible meaning
C) Interpret it to avoid impossibility or consign its meaning to God
D) Always interpret literally

Key Takeaway
When interpreting divine attributes, we must avoid impossibility by either reinterpreting or consigning the meaning to God, while affirming the apparent text.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage says it is obligatory to turn the meaning to what is not impossible for God, or to consign it. Have you ever been guilty of casually attributing human-like qualities to God in your thoughts or speech, without guarding against impossibility? What specific changes will you make in how you think about these attributes?
?
Believing in the apparent meaning of revelation while preserving transcendence requires humility. Are you willing to admit that you may not fully understand some of God's attributes, and to consign the true meaning to Him, even when it feels uncomfortable? What is one attribute you struggle with, and how can you practice tafwid or proper ta'wil starting today?

Why Logical Impossibilities Do Not Limit God's Power
Rational argument defending God from the charge of impotence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's inability to create contradictory states like motion and stillness simultaneously in one body is not a sign of weakness but a logical impossibility.

Core Idea
Logical impossibilities are not limitations on divine power.

This lesson tackles a subtle objection: if God is all-powerful, why can't He make a body both moving and still at the same time? The author argues that such a state is inherently impossible, not because God lacks power, but because it is a contradiction. This distinction is crucial for understanding divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the key terms: محال, متضادات, استعانة, منيع.
The passage uses an analogy to clarify the point.
2
Identify the main argument
The author argues that just as it is impossible for a thing to be united with its opposite, so it is impossible for God to create contradictory accidents in one subject.
The argument centers on logical impossibility, not lack of power.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that such impossibilities should not be considered as prevention (منيع), objection (احتجاج), or seeking help (استعانة).
This protects divine transcendence from anthropomorphic limitations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بدون خلق مماسة أخرى في جسم آخر، لاستحالة اجتماع الشيء مع ما يفارقه ومفارقته لما يجامعه وهذا كله محال فكذا الأول. وكذا يقال كما أن الله تعالى لا يستعين بشيء لا يمنعه أيضا شيء، فينبغي أن يقال إنه تعالى لو خلق في محل سكونا لكان قادرا أن يخلق في محل السكون الحركة ويجوز أن يخلق فيصير الجسم متحركا ساكنا في حالة واحدة وكذا في جميع المتضادات، وحيث كان هذا مستحيلا، دل أن الآخر مستحيل، وما هذا سبيله لا يعد منيعا ولا احتجاجا ولا استعانة. والله الموفق.
Without creating contact in another body, because it is impossible for a thing to be united with what separates from it and to separate from what it unites with, and all of this is impossible, so likewise the first. And likewise it is said: just as God Almighty does not seek help from anything, nothing also prevents Him. So it should be said that if He created stillness in a place, He would be able to create motion in the place of stillness, and it would be permissible for Him to create such that the body becomes moving and still in one state, and likewise in all opposites. And since this is impossible, it indicates that the other is impossible. And what is of this nature is not considered prevention, nor objection, nor seeking help. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage is an argument defending God's power from the objection that He cannot create two contradictory accidents simultaneously. The author begins by stating that creating contact without creating another body is impossible because a thing cannot be united with its opposite. He parallels this to the case of motion and stillness: if God were to create stillness in a body, He would be able to create motion in that same body, but if He created both simultaneously, the body would be both moving and still, which is impossible. Since this is impossible, the failure to create such a state does not indicate any deficiency in God's power. Rather, it is a logical impossibility, not a sign of weakness or need for assistance. The author concludes that such a case should not be considered as prevention or objection against God.

Key Terms
محال
متضادات
استعانة
منيع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's inability to create logical contradictions (like a moving and still body) proves His power is limited.
Correction: The passage shows that such contradictions are impossible by nature, not due to a deficiency in God's power. God's omnipotence extends only to what is logically possible; the impossible is not an object of power.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does God not create both motion and stillness in the same body simultaneously?
A) Because He lacks the power to do so
B) Because it would require seeking help from another
C) Because it is logically impossible for a body to be moving and still at the same time
D) Because stillness and motion are not opposites

Key Takeaway
Logical impossibilities are not limitations on God's power; they are inherent impossibilities that do not affect divine omnipotence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation that seems impossible to resolve, do you truly believe that God's power is not limited by your perception of impossibility? Will you surrender that situation to Him today, trusting that He can act within the realm of real possibilities?
?
Have you ever limited God in your heart by thinking, 'This cannot happen, so even God cannot do it'? How does this lesson challenge you to rethink the boundaries you place on divine power?
?
Is there an area of your life where you have seen God's power at work that seemed logically impossible? How can you use that memory to strengthen your trust in His absolute ability without expecting contradictions?

Divine Transcendence
intermediate

Why the Attribute is Not a Part of God
Refutes the claim that if an attribute is not other than God's essence, it would be a part.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the divine attribute, being neither identical to nor other than God's essence, does not imply that it is a part of God.

Core Idea
Being a part requires composition; God is not composed.

It might seem plausible that if an attribute is neither the essence itself nor entirely separate, it must be a part. But this reasoning relies on a flawed definition of 'part'. The author shows that 'part' in the witnessed world means a component of a composite whole, and this meaning simply does not apply to God's attributes. He then reinforces the point with a powerful analogy: something that is neither substance nor accident is considered a body in the world, but God is neither yet not a body, because 'body' is defined by composition. Likewise, the attribute is not a part because part implies composition.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection presented
Read the first sentence stating the claim: if the attribute is not other than God and not His essence, it must be a part. Notice the analogy from the witnessed world: the unit among ten and the hand of a person are neither the whole nor other.
This is the claim the author refutes.
2
Identify the refutation's core
Find the sentence starting with 'هذا كلام فاسد' (This speech is corrupt). Understand why the author rejects the analogy: a part is defined not by being neither the thing nor other, but by being a component (juz') that composes a whole along with other parts.
The key concept is tarkib (composition).
3
Follow the parallel with body
Read the analogy about body (jism): in the world, what is neither substance nor accident is a body. But God, though neither substance nor accident, is not a body because He is not composed. The same logic applies to the attribute: it is not a part because composition is absent.
This strengthens the argument by showing definition matters.
4
Synthesize the conclusion
Combine the arguments: the attribute is not other than God (because that would require two separate existents), not identical to His essence (because that would erase distinction), and not a part (because part requires composition, which does not apply to God).
5
Reflect on the deeper principle
Consider why this matters for Islamic theology: it preserves God's simplicity (tanzih) and avoids making Him composed of parts, which would imply contingency.
This connects to broader tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[الصفة ليست بعضا الله تعالى]: وما يقولون: إن الصفة لو لم تكن غير الله تعالى ولا ذاته لكانت بعضه، لأن في الشاهد ما ليس بذات شيء ولا غيره كان بعضا كالواحد من العشرة واليد من الآدمي. هذا كلام فاسد لأن البعض ما كان بعضا لأنه ليس بشيء ولا غيره بل لأنه جزء، وتركب الكل منه ومن غيره، ولم يوجد هذا المعنى في الصفة، فكما لم تكن الصفة غيره لانعدام حد الغيرين ولم تكن ذاته للاستحالة، لم تكن بعضه لانعدام حد البعض. هذا كما أن ما ليس بجوهر ولا عرض فهو جسم في الشاهد والباري جل وعلا ليس بجوهر ولا عرض ومع هذا ليس بجسم، لأن الجسم لم يكن جسما لأنه ليس بجوهر ولا عرض بل كان جسما لأنه متركب، فلما لم يكن متركبا لم يكن جسما فكذا هذا والله الموفق.
[The attribute is not a part of God Almighty]: And what they say: that if the attribute were not other than God Almighty and not His essence, it would be a part of Him, because in the witnessed world, what is not the essence of a thing nor other than it is a part, like the unit among ten and the hand of a human. This speech is corrupt, because a part is not a part because it is not a thing nor other, but rather because it is a component (juz'), and the composition (tarkib) of the whole from it and from its other, and this meaning is not found in the attribute. So just as the attribute was not other than Him due to the absence of the boundary of otherness, and was not His essence due to impossibility, it was not a part of Him due to the absence of the boundary of a part. This is like what is not a substance nor an accident: it is a body (jism) in the witnessed world, but the Creator, Glorious and Exalted, is not a substance nor an accident, yet with this He is not a body, because the body was not a body because it is not a substance nor accident, but rather it was a body because it is composed (mutarakib). So since He is not composed, He is not a body - likewise this, and God is the Giver of success.
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that if the attribute is neither identical to the essence nor entirely other, it must be a part (ba'd) of God. The objectors draw an analogy from the witnessed world: the unit among ten and the hand of a human are neither the whole nor entirely other, so they are parts. Al-Nasafi argues this is invalid because being a part is not defined by being neither the thing nor other, but by being a component from which a whole is composed. The attribute lacks this compositional meaning; it does not combine with another to make a whole. He reinforces with an analogy: in the world, what is neither substance nor accident is considered a body, but God is neither substance nor accident yet is not a body because a body is defined by composition, not by lacking substance and accident. Similarly, the attribute is not a part because part implies composition of a whole.

Key Terms
بعض
جزء
تركيب
جسم
شاهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something is neither identical to a thing nor entirely other than it, it must be a part of that thing.
Correction: A part is defined by being a component in a composite whole, not by the negation of identity and otherness. The attribute lacks this component status because God is not composed.

Checkpoint
Why is the attribute not considered a part (ba'd) of God, according to this passage?
A) Because it is other than God.
B) Because it is identical to God.
C) Because part implies composition, and God is not composed.
D) Because the attribute is not witnessed.

Key Takeaway
The attribute is not a part of God because being a part requires being a component of a composite whole, and God is not composite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever unconsciously pictured God as having 'parts'—like a personality with separate emotions or a being with distinct attributes that could be separated? How does this lesson reveal that such a picture diminishes His oneness, and what specific thought pattern will you change starting today?
?
The analogy shows that God's attributes are not like the parts of a human (hand, heart) nor like units in a number. When you describe God's mercy and wrath, do you sometimes treat them as if they are separate parts that can conflict within Him? Commit to one specific way you will remind yourself that these attributes are His essence, not parts, in your daily remembrance.
